Al-Sunnah as Source
intermediate
8 min
The Hanafi Position: Khabar al-Wahid Yields No Certain Knowledge
Stating the Hanafi position and refuting alternative views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that khabar al-wahid does not yield ʿilm, and identify the refutation of the absolute certainty view.

Core Idea
Khabar al-wahid is inherently probable; certainty is impossible with probability.

This lesson introduces the foundational Hanafi usul stance on the epistemic value of single reports. It begins by stating the majority position shared by the three imams, then critiques Ibn al-Hajib's restriction on qara'in and refutes the claim that a just narrator's report yields certain knowledge. The central argument is that a single report remains probable, and no amount of external evidence can transform it into certainty unless the evidence itself is decisive, in which case the knowledge comes from the evidence, not the report.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
List the key claim: khabar al-wahid does not yield certain knowledge (ʿilm) at all, whether or not it is accompanied by supporting evidence (qara'in).
The phrase 'with or without qara'in' is crucial.
2
Understand the critique of Ibn al-Hajib
Ibn al-Hajib restricted qara'in to incidental (zā'idah) ones. The text argues this is baseless because inherent (lāzimah) qara'in also do not produce certainty; the disagreement is about the principle itself, not the type of qarīnah.
The text suggests Ibn al-Hajib's restriction is either logically unsupported or misdirected.
3
Examine the refutation of absolute certainty from a just narrator
The view attributed to Imam Ahmad (in one narration) that any just narrator's report yields certainty is dismissed as 'manifest obstinacy'. The text cites Imam Fakhr al-Islam's reasoning: observation shows many reports are uncertain, and probability precludes certainty.
The argument from inherent probability (iḥtimāl) is a cornerstone of the Hanafi position.
4
Grasp the argument from inherent probability
A single report is always subject to possibility of error. Certainty cannot coexist with probability. Therefore, no khabar al-wahid can yield ʿilm.
This applies regardless of the narrator's apparent trustworthiness.

وتقييد ابن الحاجب بالزائدة مما لا وجه له فإنه لو كان مقصوده أن القرينة اللازمة لا يحصل معها العلم فلا يساعد دليله لعمومه وإن أراد أن النزاع فيه وأما اللازمة فلا نزاع فيها، فهذا أيضاً كما ترى، اللهم إلا أن يقال : التقييد بها لإخراج خبر المعصوم (وقيل: خبر) الواحد (العدل يفيد) العلم (مطلقاً) محفوفاً بالقرائن أولاً (فعن الإمام (أحمد) رضي الله عنه هذا الحكم (مطرد فيكون كلما أخبر العدل حصل العلم، وهذا بالقرائن، وإلا فنقول : (إن دلّت القرينة) على تحقق مضمون الخبر قطعاً، كالعلم بخجل الخجل ووجل الوجل) الحاصلين من مشاهدة الحمرة والصفرة فالعلم بها أي بالقرينة دون الخبر (وإن) دلت القرينة عليه (ظناً) إشارة إلى أنه يمكن أن يقال : العلم بالقرائن بشرط الخبر، فيحصل العلم بالمجموع، وفيه ما فيه كذا في «الحاشية». ووجه الضعف فيه أن هذا إنما يرد لو كان غرض المستدل أن كل واحد منهما لا يفيد العلم فلا علم إنما هو على تقدير تكون الإفادة على طريق الكسب، أما إذا كانت على وجه الضرورة ،فلا، بل يجوز أن يحصل بأحدهما ظن ثم يتقوى هذا الظن بظن آخر حتى يعدّ الذهن لقبول اليقين، كما يكون في المتواتر بعينه فتأمل . ثم إنه لا يرتاب المنصف أن وجود قرينة دالة على صدق المخبر قطعاً مما تمجه الضرورة الغير المكذوبة، وكذا ليس الإفادة ههنا ضرورة ، أصلاً، بل لو كانت ضرورة لكانت في خفاء البتة، ومن الأوليات أنه لا يزول الخفاء بحيث يصل إلى الجزم من الظنين، وذلك ظاهر لمن له أدنى انصاف، وإن لم ينفع للمجادل فتأمل. واستدل) في المشهور (لو أفاد خبر الواحد العلم الأدى إلى التناقض إذا أخبر عدلان بمتناقضين) إذ لو أفاد لاطرد، إذ تخصيص البعض دون البعض تحكم ولو اطرد لأفاد هذان المتناقضان العلم أيضاً، فيلزم
Chapter on Reports of Individuals. Issue: The majority of usul scholars, including the three imams, hold that a single report, if the reporter is not infallible (a prophet), does not yield certain knowledge at all, whether accompanied by supporting evidence (qara'in) or not. And it is said that a single report from a non-infallible does yield knowledge with supporting evidence, whether the evidence is incidental or inherent. Ibn al-Hajib's restriction to incidental evidence is baseless, because if he meant that inherent evidence does not produce knowledge, his proof does not support him due to its generality; if he meant that the dispute is about incidental evidence while inherent evidence is not disputed, this is also problematic, unless it is said that the restriction is to exclude the report of the infallible. And it is said that a single report from a just narrator yields knowledge absolutely, whether accompanied by evidence or not. This is reported from Imam Ahmad (may Allah be pleased with him) as a consistent ruling: whenever a just narrator reports, knowledge results. This is far from him, for it is manifest obstinacy. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said: 'As for the claim of certain knowledge, it is clearly false, because observation refutes it. I have already explained that the well-known (mashhur) does not necessitate certain knowledge, so this is even more so. This is because a single report is inherently probable, and there is no certainty with probability. Whoever denies this has degraded himself and misled his intellect.' And it is said that this ruling is not consistent; rather it may yield knowledge in some exceptional cases as a divine grace (karama). This is also invalid because it is arbitrary. We say: a report accompanied by evidence does not yield knowledge. For if the evidence indicates the truth of the content with certainty, like the knowledge of the shame of the ashamed and the fear of the fearful arising from seeing redness and paleness, then the knowledge is from the evidence, not the report. And if the evidence indicates it with probability, and the report also indicates its truth with probability, then from two probable indications, one from the evidence and one from the report, certainty does not necessarily follow. The unattended report (ghayr al-mahfuf) is not addressed because the claimant's case is obvious, needing no clarification. There is an indication that it could be said: knowledge from the evidence is conditioned by the report, so knowledge comes from the combination. There is discussion in the footnotes. The weakness is that this objection would only arise if the arguer intended that each individually does not yield knowledge, so there is no knowledge here; but the arguer's proof is for the non-attainment of knowledge at all, from the non-attainment of knowledge from two probabilities, so understand. Then you may say regarding the original argument: perhaps the evidence only indicates the truthfulness of the reporter and the impossibility of his lying in this state, just as the evidence indicates the truthfulness of the infallible prophet always, not that it indicates the truth of the content itself, such that it would be sufficient without recourse to the report. When the evidence indicates the truthfulness of the reporter and he himself reports, knowledge of the report is certain. If you say: then one of the two alternatives must be chosen? I say: I choose that the evidence does not indicate the truth of the content with certainty, but it also does not necessarily indicate probably; rather it does not indicate it at all; it only indicates with certainty the truthfulness of the reporter. So understand. It may be said: the non-yielding of certainty by the two probabilities is on the assumption that the yielding is through acquisition (kash); if it is through necessity (darurah), then it is possible, and it is permissible for a probability to be obtained from one, then this probability is strengthened by another probability until the mind is prepared to accept certainty, as happens exactly in mutawatir. So reflect. Then an unbiased person will not doubt that the existence of an evidence indicating the truthfulness of the reporter with certainty is repugnant to irresistible necessity (al-darurah al-mukadhdhabah). Likewise, the yielding here is not necessity at all; if it were necessity, it would be in extreme hiddenness. It is among the primary axioms that hiddenness does not disappear so as to reach certainty from two probabilities. That is clear to anyone with minimal fairness, even if it does not help the disputant. So reflect. And it is argued, in the well-known view, that if a single report yielded knowledge, it would lead to contradiction if two just narrators report contradictory things. For if it yielded knowledge, it would be consistent, because specifying some without others is arbitrary. And if it were consistent, these two contradictory reports would also yield knowledge, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the epistemic value of khabar al-wahid (a single transmitted report) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It begins by stating the majority position, shared by the three imams (presumably Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and most usul scholars: a khabar al-wahid does not produce certain knowledge (ʿilm), whether or not it is accompanied by supporting evidence (qara'in). The text then presents and refutes alternative views. One view, attributed to Imam Ahmad (in one narration), holds that a report from any just narrator yields certain knowledge absolutely. The author dismisses this as obstinacy, citing Imam Fakhr al-Islam's reasoning that observation shows such a position is false. Another view says the report may yield knowledge in some cases as a divine grace (karamah), which is arbitrary. The core argument is that a report is inherently probable (muḥtamal), so certainty is impossible. The text then examines the role of qara'in. If the evidence itself yields certainty (e.g., seeing the reporter's blushing and trembling indicates shame or fear), then the certainty comes from the evidence, not the report. If the evidence yields probability, then two probabilities still do not produce certainty. The author discusses Ibn al-Hajib's restriction of qara'in to incidental ones, criticizing it. A key argument presented is the contradiction proof: if khabar al-wahid produced certain knowledge, then two contradictory reports from equally trustworthy narrators would both have to be true, which is impossible. This is a classic Hanafi proof for the non-epistemic certainty of single reports. The passage ends with a subtle point about the possibility of certainty arising from cumulative probability (like in tawatur), but the author maintains that this is not the case for khabar al-wahid with qara'in unless the qarīnah itself is decisive. The genre is a pure usul dispute, analyzing different positions and presenting the standard Hanafi position that khabar al-wahid does not yield ʿilm.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wāḥid): A report narrated by one or a few individuals that does not reach the level of tawatur; it conveys only probable knowledge (ẓann).
القرينة (al-qarīnah): An external indication or evidence that accompanies the report and may strengthen its probability or, in rare cases, produce certainty.
الظن (al-ẓann): Probable knowledge; the epistemic status of khabar al-wahid, which falls short of certainty (yaqīn).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position stated in the text, what is the epistemic value of khabar al-wahid?
A) It yields certain knowledge when accompanied by strong evidence
B) It yields certain knowledge only if the reporter is a just narrator
C) It does not yield certain knowledge at all, with or without evidence
D) It sometimes yields certain knowledge as a divine grace

Key Takeaway
Khabar al-wahid is inherently probable (ẓannī) and never attains the level of certain knowledge (ʿilm) in the Hanafi school, regardless of accompanying evidence.

Qara'in and the Contradiction Proof: Why Single Reports Cannot Yield Certainty
Detailed analysis of qara'in and presentation of the contradiction proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi argument that two probable sources do not produce certainty, and evaluate the contradiction proof as a reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Two probabilities equal no certainty; qara'in can indicate truthfulness but not content.

This lesson dives into the detailed analysis of qara'in (supporting evidence) and the powerful contradiction proof that underpins the Hanafi stance. The text examines whether qara'in can ever elevate a khabar al-wahid to certainty, concluding that they cannot because either the qarīnah itself is certain (making the report redundant) or it is probable (so two probabilities still leave uncertainty). The lesson also presents the famous contradiction argument: if khabar al-wahid produced certainty, then two contradictory reports from equally trustworthy narrators would both have to be true, which is impossible. This logical proof is a cornerstone of Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Understand the analysis of qara'in
Distinguish between two scenarios: (a) qarīnah indicates the truth of the content with certainty (e.g., observing blush indicates shame), and (b) qarīnah indicates probability.
In scenario (a), the knowledge comes from the qarīnah, not the report.
2
Recognize the 'two probabilities' argument
If both the qarīnah and the report only provide probability, then the combination of two probabilities does not yield certainty. This is a key logical point.
The text emphasizes that 'from two probable indications, certainty does not necessarily follow.'
3
Consider the cumulative probability discussion
The text entertains the idea that multiple probabilities might strengthen each other to produce certainty via necessity (ḍarūrah), like in mutawātir. But it rejects this for khabar al-wahid with qara'in, because the evidence does not indicate content but only truthfulness.
The cumulative mechanism is denied in this context.
4
Examine the contradiction proof (dalīl al-tanāquḍ)
If every khabar al-wahid from a just narrator yielded certain knowledge, then two contradictory reports from two just narrators would both be certain, which is impossible. Therefore, the premise is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum that proves khabar al-wahid cannot yield certainty.

Chapter on Reports of Individuals. Issue: The majority of usul scholars, including the three imams, hold that a single report, if the reporter is not infallible (a prophet), does not yield certain knowledge at all, whether accompanied by supporting evidence (qara'in) or not. And it is said that a single report from a non-infallible does yield knowledge with supporting evidence, whether the evidence is incidental or inherent. Ibn al-Hajib's restriction to incidental evidence is baseless, because if he meant that inherent evidence does not produce knowledge, his proof does not support him due to its generality; if he meant that the dispute is about incidental evidence while inherent evidence is not disputed, this is also problematic, unless it is said that the restriction is to exclude the report of the infallible. And it is said that a single report from a just narrator yields knowledge absolutely, whether accompanied by evidence or not. This is reported from Imam Ahmad (may Allah be pleased with him) as a consistent ruling: whenever a just narrator reports, knowledge results. This is far from him, for it is manifest obstinacy. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said: 'As for the claim of certain knowledge, it is clearly false, because observation refutes it. I have already explained that the well-known (mashhur) does not necessitate certain knowledge, so this is even more so. This is because a single report is inherently probable, and there is no certainty with probability. Whoever denies this has degraded himself and misled his intellect.' And it is said that this ruling is not consistent; rather it may yield knowledge in some exceptional cases as a divine grace (karama). This is also invalid because it is arbitrary. We say: a report accompanied by evidence does not yield knowledge. For if the evidence indicates the truth of the content with certainty, like the knowledge of the shame of the ashamed and the fear of the fearful arising from seeing redness and paleness, then the knowledge is from the evidence, not the report. And if the evidence indicates it with probability, and the report also indicates its truth with probability, then from two probable indications, one from the evidence and one from the report, certainty does not necessarily follow. The unattended report (ghayr al-mahfuf) is not addressed because the claimant's case is obvious, needing no clarification. There is an indication that it could be said: knowledge from the evidence is conditioned by the report, so knowledge comes from the combination. There is discussion in the footnotes. The weakness is that this objection would only arise if the arguer intended that each individually does not yield knowledge, so there is no knowledge here; but the arguer's proof is for the non-attainment of knowledge at all, from the non-attainment of knowledge from two probabilities, so understand. Then you may say regarding the original argument: perhaps the evidence only indicates the truthfulness of the reporter and the impossibility of his lying in this state, just as the evidence indicates the truthfulness of the infallible prophet always, not that it indicates the truth of the content itself, such that it would be sufficient without recourse to the report. When the evidence indicates the truthfulness of the reporter and he himself reports, knowledge of the report is certain. If you say: then one of the two alternatives must be chosen? I say: I choose that the evidence does not indicate the truth of the content with certainty, but it also does not necessarily indicate probably; rather it does not indicate it at all; it only indicates with certainty the truthfulness of the reporter. So understand. It may be said: the non-yielding of certainty by the two probabilities is on the assumption that the yielding is through acquisition (kash); if it is through necessity (darurah), then it is possible, and it is permissible for a probability to be obtained from one, then this probability is strengthened by another probability until the mind is prepared to accept certainty, as happens exactly in mutawatir. So reflect. Then an unbiased person will not doubt that the existence of an evidence indicating the truthfulness of the reporter with certainty is repugnant to irresistible necessity (al-darurah al-mukadhdhabah). Likewise, the yielding here is not necessity at all; if it were necessity, it would be in extreme hiddenness. It is among the primary axioms that hiddenness does not disappear so as to reach certainty from two probabilities. That is clear to anyone with minimal fairness, even if it does not help the disputant. So reflect. And it is argued, in the well-known view, that if a single report yielded knowledge, it would lead to contradiction if two just narrators report contradictory things. For if it yielded knowledge, it would be consistent, because specifying some without others is arbitrary. And if it were consistent, these two contradictory reports would also yield knowledge, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the epistemic value of khabar al-wahid (a single transmitted report) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It begins by stating the majority position, shared by the three imams (presumably Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and most usul scholars: a khabar al-wahid does not produce certain knowledge (ʿilm), whether or not it is accompanied by supporting evidence (qara'in). The text then presents and refutes alternative views. One view, attributed to Imam Ahmad (in one narration), holds that a report from any just narrator yields certain knowledge absolutely. The author dismisses this as obstinacy, citing Imam Fakhr al-Islam's reasoning that observation shows such a position is false. Another view says the report may yield knowledge in some cases as a divine grace (karamah), which is arbitrary. The core argument is that a report is inherently probable (muḥtamal), so certainty is impossible. The text then examines the role of qara'in. If the evidence itself yields certainty (e.g., seeing the reporter's blushing and trembling indicates shame or fear), then the certainty comes from the evidence, not the report. If the evidence yields probability, then two probabilities still do not produce certainty. The author discusses Ibn al-Hajib's restriction of qara'in to incidental ones, criticizing it. A key argument presented is the contradiction proof: if khabar al-wahid produced certain knowledge, then two contradictory reports from equally trustworthy narrators would both have to be true, which is impossible. This is a classic Hanafi proof for the non-epistemic certainty of single reports. The passage ends with a subtle point about the possibility of certainty arising from cumulative probability (like in tawatur), but the author maintains that this is not the case for khabar al-wahid with qara'in unless the qarīnah itself is decisive. The genre is a pure usul dispute, analyzing different positions and presenting the standard Hanafi position that khabar al-wahid does not yield ʿilm.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wāḥid): A report narrated by one or a few individuals that does not reach the level of tawatur; it conveys only probable knowledge (ẓann).
القرينة (al-qarīnah): An external indication or evidence that accompanies the report and may strengthen its probability or, in rare cases, produce certainty.
الظن (al-ẓann): Probable knowledge; the epistemic status of khabar al-wahid, which falls short of certainty (yaqīn).
دليل التناقض (dalīl al-tanāquḍ): The argument that if khabar al-wahid yielded certainty, contradictory reports would both be true, which is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the logical consequence of the contradiction proof?
A) Khabar al-wahid can yield certainty if the narrators are just.
B) Khabar al-wahid cannot yield certainty because that would require contradictory reports to both be true.
C) Khabar al-wahid yields certainty only if there is no contradictory report.
D) The contradiction proof is irrelevant to the epistemic value of khabar al-wahid.

Key Takeaway
Khabar al-wahid remains probable (ẓannī) even with qara'in; the contradiction proof shows that attributing certainty to single reports leads to logical absurdity.

intermediate
10 min
Epistemic Value of Khabar al-Wahid with Qara'in
Dispute over whether khabar al-wahid with qara'in yields certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on khabar al-wahid with qara'in and evaluate the argument that such a report yields certainty.

Core Idea
Khabar al-wahid with qara'in yields only zann; certainty, if present, is from the qara'in alone.

This passage tackles a key usul dispute: does a single-narrator report (khabar al-wahid) ever yield certainty (ilm) when supported by strong contextual indications (qara'in)? Some scholars, attributed to Imam Ahmad, argue it does. The Hanafi school firmly rejects this, arguing that any certainty comes from the qara'in, not the report itself. This has important implications for how we weigh evidence in ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
State the opposing view: khabar al-wahid with qara'in yields certainty (ilm). Who holds this? According to the text, it is attributed to Imam Ahmad.
2
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
Examine the two main arguments: (1) If qarina gives certainty, it is independent; if both are probabilistic, combination cannot produce certainty. (2) The contradiction argument: if such reports yielded certainty, contradictory reports would force both to be true. (3) The consensus argument: no mujtahid is declared mistaken for opposing such a report.
3
Understand the distinction between qarina and report
The Hanafi response uses the king example: certainty of the son's death is from the qara'in (circumstantial evidence), not the report. Without the report, the qara'in would still indicate death of a loved one, but not specifically the son.
4
Evaluate the practical consequence
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis see such reports as probabilistic, so ijtihad against them is not automatically error. Those who claim certainty would deem opposition as error.
5
Reflect on the epistemological principle
The Hanafi position upholds that probability (zann) does not become certainty by accumulation; only tawatur yields certainty. This preserves the hierarchy of evidence.

القطع فيه بالنظر خلاف الواقع قطعاً وهو الخطأ، فحين إفاده القطع يلزمه كونه خطأ خلاف الواقع، وأن الحكم به حكم بما علم قطعاً أنه خلاف حكم الله فيفسخ، لأنه ماذا بعد الحق إلا الضلال، أنه لا يفسخ إجماعاً (وأجيب) عن الأول ( بأن المحفوف بالقرائن يستحيل) وقوعه (عادة في المتناقضين) فلا يلزم التناقض إلا على تقدير مستحيل عادة فلا استحالة، وأما غير المحفوف فنحن معكم في عدم الإفادة وتحققه في المتناقضين، وأجيب أقول : لو لم يرتفع هذا الجواز جواز موت شخص آخر بالقرائن، فارتفاعه بالخبر، و) الحال أنه (هو يحتمل الصدق والكذب محل نظر ) بل لا يرتفع بالخبر أصلاً فإنه من البين أنه لا يرتفع
And the restriction by Ibn al-Hajib to the superfluous [qara'in] has no justification, because if his intention was that the necessary qarina does not produce knowledge with it, then his proof does not support his generality; and if he intended that the dispute is about it while as for the necessary there is no dispute, then this is also as you see. Unless it is said: the restriction is to exclude the report of the infallible. (And it is said: the report of one) just (narrator yields) knowledge (absolutely), whether accompanied by qara'in or not. (Thus from Imam Ahmad, may Allah be pleased with him, this ruling is uniform, so that whenever a just narrator reports, knowledge is obtained. And this is far from his like, for it is manifest obstinacy. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said: As for the claim of certain knowledge, it is false without doubt because direct observation refutes it. And I have already shown that the well-known does not necessitate certain knowledge, so this is more fitting. This is because khabar al-wahid is inevitably probable, and there is no certainty with probability. Whoever denies this has debased himself and misled his intellect. (And it is said: this ruling is not uniform); rather it may yield in some cases as a miracle from Allah, and this is also invalid because it is arbitrary. For us, I say: the report accompanied by qara'in does not yield. Otherwise we say: if the qarina indicates the realization of the content of the report with certainty, like knowledge of the embarrassment of the embarrassed and the fear of the fearful resulting from observing redness and paleness, then knowledge is by it, i.e., by the qarina, not by the report. And if the qarina indicates it probabilistically, and the report also indicates the realization of its content probabilistically, then from two probabilistics, one obtained by the qarina and the other by the report, certainty does not necessarily follow. He did not address the report without qara'in because the claim regarding it is clear and needless of explanation. There is in it an indication that it can be said: knowledge through qara'in is conditioned by the report, so knowledge is obtained from the combination, and there is something in it. Thus in al-Hashiya. The weakness in it is that this only applies if the goal of the arguer is that each one does not yield knowledge, so there is no knowledge here. But the arguer only argued for the absence of knowledge altogether from the absence of knowledge from two probabilistics. Understand. Then you can say on the basis of the argument: perhaps the qarina only indicates the truthfulness of the reporter and the impossibility of his lying in this case, just as the qarina indicates the truthfulness of the infallible Prophet always, not that it indicates the realization of the content of the report such that it is itself sufficient without needing the report. So when the qarina indicates the truthfulness of the reporter and he himself reports, knowledge of this report is certain. If you say: then one of the two alternatives must be chosen? I say: I choose that the qarina does not indicate the realization of the content of the report with certainty, but it does not follow that it indicates probabilistically; rather it does not indicate it at all. Rather it indicates with certainty the truthfulness of the reporter. Understand. And it may be said: the non-yielding of a probabilistic for certainty is only on the assumption that the yielding is by way of acquisition; but if it is by way of necessity, then no. Rather it is possible that a probability is obtained by one, then strengthened by another probability until the mind becomes receptive to certainty, as occurs in mutawatir exactly. Reflect. Then the unbiased person does not doubt that the existence of a qarina indicating the truthfulness of the reporter with certainty is something that undeniable necessity rejects. Likewise, the yielding here is not necessity at all; rather if it were necessity, it would be in complete concealment. It is among the primary truths that concealment does not disappear such that it reaches certainty from two probabilistics. That is apparent to anyone with the least fairness, even if it does not help the disputant. Reflect. And it is argued) in the well-known (that if khabar al-wahid yielded knowledge, it would lead to contradiction if two just narrators report contradictory things), because if it yielded, it would be uniform, since specifying some without others is arbitrary. If it were uniform, these two contradictory reports would also yield knowledge, necessitating the realization of their content, which is contradiction. Thus what is in al-Hashiya is refuted: that it does not apply to those who do not hold uniformity. For if it is said: perhaps the reporting of two just narrators of two contradictory reports, though intellectually possible, is customarily impossible. He said: (and that) i.e., the report of two just narrators of contradictories (is possible), rather (it occurs, as is not hidden from one who examines the Sahihs, Sunans, and Musnads). And it may be said: if this were valid, it would indicate that khabar al-wahid does not yield probability, otherwise in this case probability of contradictories would be necessitated, which is also invalid. The solution is that knowledge is perhaps conditioned by the absence of opposition, and here opposition exists between the two reports. You can say in response: certainty is about the actual thing. So if khabar al-wahid yielded knowledge, the report would only occur where the reported thing is actual, and since knowledge of it is uniform, otherwise arbitrariness would follow. So it would not be valid for a report to occur at all except when the reported is actual in reality. Thus if reporting of contradictories occurs, their actuality is necessitated. This is unlike probability, where the reported need not be actual; rather it may be false. The report only yields probability, so if another report comes, it lifts this probability. As for knowledge, though lifting of certainty is possible just as in probability, lifting of what is actual is not valid by someone's report, since the report does not change reality, so contradiction is necessitated. This is very apparent, so take it to heart. (And) it is argued in the well-known also: if khabar al-wahid yielded knowledge, it would necessitate declaring the mujtahid who opposes the report to be mistaken, because it would be ijtihad against a decisive [proof], thus error, (and this is contrary to consensus, for no one has declared a mufti mistaken). Unlike khabar al-wahid with ijtihad, even if a judge judges contrary to ahad reports by his opinion, his judgment is not overturned at all. And it may be said: error is only necessitated if the knowledge from khabar al-wahid is necessary, but it is not so; rather the one claiming knowledge claims it as theoretical, so there is no error in opposition. Its response: that opposition to a decisive proof, even if the certainty in it is theoretical, is certainly contrary to reality, and that is error. So when it yields certainty, it necessitates that [the opponent] is in error contrary to reality, and ruling by it is ruling by what is known with certainty to be contrary to Allah's ruling, so it is overturned, because what after truth except error? But it is not overturned by consensus. (And it is answered) regarding the first (that the report accompanied by qara'in is customarily impossible) to occur in contradictions, so contradiction is only necessitated on a customarily impossible assumption, so no impossibility. As for the report without qara'in, we agree with you on its non-yielding and its occurrence in contradictions. And regarding the second, it is answered that error is only necessitated if the accompanied report occurs in legal matters, (and it does not occur in legal matters; and if it occurred) hypothetically and suppositionally, (we would declare the opponent mistaken) and overturn the ruling by it. Yes, the proof is valid for the unaccompanied report, and we agree with you on it, understand. : Those who claim the accompanied report is decisive (said: if a king is informed of the death of his son while he was in the throes of death with screaming, violation of sanctity, and the like, we would be certain of its truth; thus the accompanied report yields certainty. (We say: the knowledge) obtained there is by the mentioned qara'in, (not by the report). And if we suppose the report removed from the picture, the knowledge remains as it was. (And it is answered that without the report we would have considered the possibility of another person dying, for the mentioned qara'in only indicate the death of the king's relatives and loved ones; as for the specific son, it is by the addition of the report. (Thus in al-Mukhtasar; I say: if this possibility of another person dying by the qara'in is not removed, its removal is by the report, and) while it (is subject to truth and falsehood, it is questionable) rather it is not removed by the report at all, for it is obvious that it is not removed.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
The passage is a detailed discussion of the epistemological value of khabar al-wahid (a single-narrator report) when accompanied by contextual indications (qara'in). The author, as a Hanafi commentator, engages with the view that such reports can yield certainty (ilm), a position attributed to Imam Ahmad and others. He refutes this by analyzing the role of qara'in. The core argument is that if a qarina indicates the truthfulness of the reporter with certainty, the certainty comes from the qarina, not the report itself. If both the qarina and the report are probabilistic, the combination still cannot produce certainty. The author then presents two well-known arguments: (1) the contradiction argument — if khabar al-wahid yielded certainty, then two contradictory reports from just narrators would force both to be true, which is impossible; (2) the consensus argument — if such a report yielded certainty, any mujtahid opposing it would be declared mistaken, but consensus holds that no mujtahid is declared mistaken for opposing a khabar al-wahid. The author responds that the accompanied report (mahfuf) is customarily impossible in contradictory cases, and in legal matters such reports do not actually occur. The discussion concludes that the knowledge from an accompanied report comes from the qara'in, not the report, and that the report alone remains probabilistic. This is a classic Hanafi position: khabar al-wahid, even with qara'in, does not produce certainty; it only yields probability (zann). The text uses the example of a king learning of his son's death — the certainty comes from the circumstantial evidence, not the informant's word.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A report transmitted by one or a few narrators that does not reach the level of tawatur.
محفوف بالقرائن (mahfuf bi al-qara'in): A report accompanied by contextual indications (qara'in) that support its truthfulness or content.
ظن (zann): Probabilistic belief, less than certainty, subject to error.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the source of certainty when a khabar al-wahid is accompanied by strong contextual indications (qara'in)?
A) The report itself, because it is strengthened by the qara'in.
B) The qara'in independently, not the report.
C) Both the report and the qara'in jointly produce certainty.
D) Neither the report nor the qara'in can produce certainty ever.

Key Takeaway
Khabar al-wahid, even with strong qara'in, yields only probability (zann) in Hanafi usul; certainty must come from independent mutawatir or necessary knowledge.

Role of Contextual Indications (Qara'in) in Khabar al-Wahid
Analysis of qara'in and certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qara'in thubut and qara'in sidq, and evaluate their capacity to produce certainty from khabar al-wahid in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Contextual indications can produce certainty only if definitive; for non-Ma'sum, such qara'in are practically unavailable.

In this passage, the author explains the nuanced epistemology of khabar al-wahid when accompanied by contextual clues. The key distinction is between qara'in that confirm the report's content (thubut al-mazmun) and those that confirm the reporter's truthfulness (sidq al-mukhbit). Understanding this framework is essential for Hanafi usul, as it clarifies when solitary reports can rise to the level of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of qara'in
The passage distinguishes between qara'in that confirm the content of the report (e.g., knowing the son is dying and hearing screams) and qara'in that confirm the truthfulness of the reporter (e.g., the reporter's known reliability).
This distinction determines whether the qara'in affect thubut or sidq.
2
Analyze the effect of definitive vs. non-definitive qara'in
If qara'in are definitive (qat'i), they produce certainty and the report becomes irrelevant. If non-definitive, the possibility of error remains, so only zann is achieved.
Non-definitive qara'in cannot remove the inherent probability of error in the khabar.
3
Identify the exception for infallible reporters
For the Prophet (ﷺ) and the consensus community (ahl al-ijma'), definitive qara'in are theoretically possible. For others, the passage asserts that such qara'in are not verifiable, leaving khabar al-wahid in the realm of zann.
This is a core Hanafi position limiting the epistemic value of solitary reports.
4
Grasp the distinction between conviction and true knowledge
Even strong conviction (jasm) may not constitute true knowledge ('ilm) if the possibility of error exists. The king example illustrates how conviction can be overturned by new information.
Certainty requires that no contradictory possibility remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من احتمال النقيض بما يحتمل الكذب ،البتة وقد يقال إنه لو علم إشراف الولد بخصوصه على الموت بعروض الغرغرة وغيرها ثم وجدت هذه الأحوال من الصراخ وتهتك الحرم فالعلم بالقرائن، ولا دخل فيه للخبر أصلاً كما في حمرة الخجل وأما لو علم إشراف أحد الأقارب ثم وجدت هذه الأحوال فلا تفيد هذه القرائن إلا موت أحد من الأقارب، وبالخبر يتعين موت الولد ولا يذهب عليك أنه إذا لم تفد القرائن موت الولد بخصوصه فالإخبار الذي يحتمل الكذب لا يعين موت الولد فافهم والقول الفصل أن القرائن إن كانت قرائن ثبوت مضمون الخبر كما في المثال المضروب فإن كانت قاطعة فيحصل العلم بها، ويلغوا لخبر وإن كانت غير قاطعة فمعها يبقى احتمال عدم ثبوت مضمون الخبر والإخبار أيضاً يحتمل عدم ثبوت مضمونه، فلا يرتفع هذا الاحتمال من البين فلا قطع وإن كانت القرائن قرائن صدق المخبر، فإن كانت دالة عليه قطعاً، فإذا أخبر مع وجود تلك القرائن حصل القطع بصدق الخبر وتحقق مضمونه قطعاً، لكن الكلام في تحقق هذه القرائن في غير المعصوم من النبي وأهل الإجماع فإنه لم يدل دليل على تحققها في مادة من المواد فلا بد من إثبات تحققها ودونه خرط القتاد، هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم هذا المقام ثم أنه ربما يجاب عن دليلهم بأن غاية ما لزم منه ثبوت الجزم، وأما كونه علماً فلا لجواز عدم مطابقة الخبر، وكون الجزم جهلاً مركباً، إلا ترى أنه لو أخبر الملك بعد هذا الخبر بأنه لم يمت وإنما اشتبه الحال زال الجزم بالموت. كذا في «الحاشية» فتأمل فيه الطاردون للقطع
From the possibility of the opposite, including the possibility of lying, absolutely. And it may be said that if one specifically knows that his son is approaching death due to the occurrence of gurgling etc., then these conditions of screaming and tearing of sanctity occur, then the knowledge is from the contextual indications, and the report has no role at all, like the redness of shyness. But if one knows that a relative is approaching death, then these conditions only indicate the death of one of the relatives; by the report, the death of the son is specifically determined. And it does not escape you that if the contextual indications do not indicate the death of the son specifically, then the report that could be false does not specify the death of the son, so understand. And the decisive statement is that if the contextual indications are indications of the truth of the content of the report, as in the example given, then if they are definitive, knowledge is obtained by them, and the report is disregarded. If they are not definitive, then with them the possibility that the content of the report is not established remains, and the report also carries that possibility, so this possibility is not removed, so there is no certainty. And if the contextual indications are indications of the truthfulness of the reporter, then if they indicate that definitively, if the report comes with those indications, certainty of the truth of the report is obtained and its content is realized definitively. But the discussion is about the realization of these indications in other than the infallible (the Prophet and people of consensus), for there is no evidence that they are realized in any particular matter. So it is necessary to prove their realization, and that is extremely difficult. This is how this station should be understood. Then it is possible to respond to their proof that the most it entails is the existence of conviction, but it is not knowledge because of the possibility that the report does not match reality, and conviction could be compound ignorance. Do you not see that if the king informed after this report that he did not die and that the matter was confused, the conviction of death would leave. Thus in al-Hashiya. So reflect on it, O those who expel certainty.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut discusses the role of context (qara'in) in achieving certainty from khabar al-wahid. The author gives an example: if someone knows his son is about to die and then hears screaming, the context alone gives knowledge of the son's death, not the report. But if the knowledge is only of a relative's death, context only tells that some relative died; the report specifies which one. The author distinguishes two types of qara'in: those that confirm the content (thubut al-mazmun) and those that confirm the reporter's truthfulness (sidq al-mukhbit). If definitive, they produce certainty. However, for non-Ma'sum (other than the Prophet and the consensus community), such definitive qara'in are practically unavailable. Thus, khabar al-wahid without such qara'in remains zanni. The author also notes that even if one has conviction, it may not be true knowledge, as illustrated by a king's counter-information. This is a classic Hanafi usul position: khabar al-wahid does not produce certainty unless accompanied by qara'in.

Key Terms
القرائن (al-qara'in): Contextual indications that accompany a report and may produce certainty or strengthen zann
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A report transmitted by one or a few narrators not reaching tawatur
قرائن ثبوت (qara'in thubut): Contextual indications that confirm the content of the report (e.g., sensory evidence)
قرائن صدق (qara'in sidq): Contextual indications that confirm the truthfulness of the reporter (e.g., known reliability)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, when do contextual indications (qara'in) produce certainty from a solitary report?
A) Whenever they are present in any form
B) Only if they are definitive and confirm either the content or the reporter's truthfulness
C) Only if the report comes from a trustworthy narrator
D) Never, because all reports are zanni

Key Takeaway
Contextual indications can elevate a solitary report to certainty only when they are definitive and verifiable, a condition rarely met outside the infallible.

Hanafi Refutation: Acting on Khabar al-Wahid Despite Zann
Refuting the argument that ijma' obligates acting on khabar al-wahid despite zann

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four Hanafi responses to the objection that acting on khabar al-wahid violates the Quranic prohibition of following assumption, and apply the principle that ijma' can make zanni evidence obligatory.

Core Idea
Acting on khabar al-wahid is based on definitive ijma', not on its zann.

This passage presents a classic debate in Usul al-Fiqh: how can we act on a lone narrator report (khabar al-wahid) when it only produces probability (zann), and the Quran forbids following assumption? The Hanafi school defends the obligation to act on such reports by appealing to the consensus (ijma') of the Companions and later scholars, which is a definitive proof. In this lesson, we will examine the opponent's argument and the four-part Hanafi response that clarifies the relationship between zann, ijma', and the Quranic text.

Guided Path
1
State the opponent's argument
The opponent argues that khabar al-wahid is zanni, and since the Quran prohibits following assumption, acting on it would be haram. Yet they claim it is obligatory by ijma'.
2
First Hanafi response: action based on ijma'
The author argues that we do not follow the zann itself; we follow the ijma' which commands action. Ijma' is qat'i, so the prohibition does not apply.
3
Second response: prohibition limited to usul al-din
The verses prohibiting zann are specific to matters of belief (usul al-din), not practical matters (furu'). In furu', zann is acceptable, as seen with the apparent meaning of the Quran which is also zanni.
4
Third response: reductio ad absurdum
If the opponent's reasoning were valid, it would also invalidate ra'y (personal opinion), which is accepted by ijma'. This shows inconsistency.
5
Fourth response: reinterpretation of the verses
The verses may be addressed to the Prophet specifically, or 'knowledge' may include zann, or the prohibition is for abandoning certainty for assumption, not for following zann per se.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: يجب العمل به أي بخبر الواحد العدل (إجماعاً) ولو لم يكن مفيداً للعلم لما وجب العمل به بل حرم كيف وقد قال تعالى: ﴿وَلَا نَقْفُ مَا لَيسَ لَكَ بِهِ عِلم [الإسراء: ٣٦] وقد نهى عن اتباع ما ليس له به علم (و) قال تعالى : إن يَتَّبِعُونَ إِلَّا الظَّنَّ ﴾ [الأنعام: ١١٦] [يونس : ٦٦] [النجم: ٢٣ و٢٨] وهو ذم على اتباع الظن فيحرم (قلنا : أولاً) ليس المتبع في العمل بخبر الواحد الظن الحاصل به حتى يكون منهياً عنه، بل (المتبع) هناك (الإجماع) الدال على العمل به هو قاطع فلا يلزم العمل بالظن المحض (كذا في «المختصر وتبعه بعض شراح أصول الإمام فخر الإسلام قدس سره (أقول : الظاهر أنه إجماع على العمل به أي الخبر فيكون العمل بالظن (لا) أنه عمل بالإجماع) حتى يكون العمل بالقاطع بدليل العمل به في حياته عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام) ولا إجماع هناك، فالإجماع دليل على العمل بالخبر ، فلو لم يكن مفيداً للعلم لزم الإجماع على خلاف النص القاطع (و) قلنا (ثانياً) تحريم العمل بالظن المدلول عليه بالكريمتين (مخصوص بأصول الدين فإن الظن واجب الاعتبار في العمليات بالدلائل القاطعة، إلا ترى أنه يجب العمل بظاهر الكتاب مع كونه مظنوناً (و) قلنا (ثالثاً كما أقول لو تم ما ذكرتم (لدل على بطلان الرأي وأفاد العلم لأن الرأي مظنون فيحرم اتباعه للكريمتين أو نقول الرأي واجب العمل إجماعاً فلو لم يفد العلم للزم الظن وهو منهي بالكريمتين وقلنا رابعاً: لا نسلم تحريم العمل بالظن، والكريمتان لا تدلان عليه أصلاً، أما الأولى فلأنه خطاب للرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم، ولا يلزم من حرمة اتباع الظن له مع كونه قادراً على تحصيل اليقين بالانتظار إلى الوحي الحرمة لنا مع عدم قدرتنا وأيضاً : يحتمل أن يراد بالعلم مطلق التصديق الشامل للظن، فإن إطلاق العلم عليه ،شائع، وأيضاً يجوز أن يراد بما ليس بعلم ما يكون خلافه معلوماً، وكذا الجواب لو استدل بقوله تعالى خطاباً لنوح عليه الصلاة والسلام وعلى نبينا وعلى آله وأصحابه الكرام : ﴿فَلَا تَسْتَلْنِ مَا لَيْسَ لَكَ بِهِ عِلْمٌ ﴾ [هود : ٤٦] وأما الثانية : لأن الذم فيها ليس لاتباع الظن بل لانحصار حالهم في اتباع الظن وعدم اتباعهم إلا الظن، ولا شك أنه مذموم، لأن فيه ترك ما هو معلوم قطعاً فافهم
They said: Acting upon it, i.e., the solitary report of a trustworthy narrator, is obligatory by consensus. If it were not beneficial for knowledge, it would not be obligatory to act upon it; rather it would be forbidden. How (could it be obligatory) when Allah said: 'And do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge' [Al-Isra: 36], and He prohibited following that of which one has no knowledge. And He said: 'They follow nothing but assumption' [Al-An'am: 116, Yunus: 66, An-Najm: 23, 28], and that is blame for following assumption, so it is forbidden. We respond: First, the follower in acting upon the solitary report does not follow the assumption obtained from it such that he would be prohibited; rather the follower there is the consensus indicating the obligation to act upon it, which is definitive, so it is not following pure assumption. (Thus in al-Mukhtasar, and some commentators of the principles of Imam Fakhr al-Islam followed him. I say: It is apparent that it is consensus on acting upon the report, so acting is upon assumption, not that it is acting upon consensus, such that the action is upon a definitive proof, due to the evidence of acting upon it in his lifetime, peace be upon him and his family. And there is no consensus there. So consensus is a proof for acting upon the report. If it were not beneficial for knowledge, then consensus would be contrary to a definitive text. Second, the prohibition of acting upon assumption indicated by the two noble verses is specific to the principles of religion (usul al-din), for assumption is necessarily considered in practical matters (furū') due to definitive proofs. Do you not see that it is obligatory to act upon the apparent meaning of the Book despite it being assumed? Third, as I say, if what you mentioned were valid, it would indicate the invalidity of opinion (ra'y) and produce knowledge, because opinion is assumed, so following it would be prohibited by the two verses. Or we say: Opinion is obligatory to act upon by consensus, so if it did not produce knowledge, it would entail assumption which is prohibited by the two verses. Fourth, we do not accept the prohibition of acting upon assumption. The two noble verses do not indicate it at all. As for the first verse, it is an address to the Messenger, peace be upon him and his family, and the prohibition of his following assumption while he was able to obtain certainty by waiting for revelation does not entail prohibition for us when we are unable. Also, it is possible that 'knowledge' means absolute affirmation which includes assumption, for the usage of 'knowledge' for it is common. Also, it is possible that what is meant by 'that of which you have no knowledge' is that whose opposite is known. Similarly, the answer if one uses the verse addressed to Noah, peace be upon him: 'So do not ask Me for that of which you have no knowledge' [Hud: 46]. As for the second verse, the blame in it is not for following assumption, but for the fact that they restrict themselves to following assumption and do not follow anything else, and there is no doubt that this is blameworthy because it involves abandoning what is decisively known. So understand.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage records a classic debate on whether khabar al-wahid should be acted upon despite being zanni (assumptive). The opponents (likely Shafi'is or traditionalists) argue that ijma' obligates action, and that acting on zann is prohibited by Quranic verses. The Hanafi author gives four responses: 1) The action is based on following ijma', which is qat'i, not the zann itself. 2) The prohibition of zann applies only to usul al-din, not to furu'. 3) If the argument were valid, it would also condemn ra'y (opinion), which is accepted by ijma'. 4) The Quranic verses are not general prohibitions of zann; they are specific contexts or interpreted differently. This shows the Hanafi usul principle that zann in practical matters is valid when supported by qat'i dalil, such as ijma'.

Key Terms
الإجماع (al-ijma'): Consensus of the mujtahidun of the ummah on a ruling, considered a qat'i proof
الظن (al-zann): Strong probability, less than certainty; in usul, it is the basis for action in furu'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first Hanafi response, what is the actual basis for acting on khabar al-wahid?
A) The zann produced by the report
B) The definitive consensus of the ummah
C) The apparent meaning of the Quran
D) The reporter's trustworthiness

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defends acting on zanni reports by anchoring the obligation on decisive ijma', not on the zann itself.

Refutation of Ibn al-Salah's Claim on Sahihayn Producing Certainty
Refuting the claim that Sahihayn yield certain knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate Ibn al-Salah's claim that Sahihayn produce certain knowledge, applying the Hanafi distinction between zann and ilm.

Core Idea
No hadith collection, even Sahihayn, produces certain knowledge; only strong assumption (zann qawi) is yielded.

Can we treat every hadith in Bukhari and Muslim as definitively true? Some scholars like Ibn al-Salah argued that the ummah's consensus on their authenticity elevates them to certain knowledge. In this passage, our author dismantles that claim, showing that authenticity and certainty are distinct, and that even the most rigorous collections only yield strong probability. Understanding this distinction is vital for proper juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its basis
Locate Ibn al-Salah's assertion that Sahihayn produce theoretical certainty (ilm nazari) and note his evidence: alleged consensus on their superiority and acceptance by the ummah.
The claim is that ijma on their merit makes their content certain.
2
Analyze the Hanafi refutation
Break down the author's counter-arguments: common sense, contradictory narrations, lack of consensus on all narrators (including Qadaris), and the distinction between superior conditions and certainty.
Focus on the logical structure: the claimed ijma is not established.
3
Evaluate the epistemological consequence
Understand what level of certainty (if any) the Hanafis assign: only strong assumption (zann qawi) because the narrations are subject to further scrutiny.
Knowledge (ilm) requires more than even the best khabar al-wahid.
4
Assess the tarjih (preferred view)
Recognize that the author sides with Ibn al-Humam and the majority of jurists, rejecting the superiority claim as arbitrary unless narrators are equally reliable.
The preferred view is that al-asahiyya depends on individual narrator conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع ابن الصلاح وطائفة من الملقبين بأهل الحديث زعموا أن رواية الشيخين محمد ابن إسماعيل البخاري ومسلم بن الحجاج صاحبي الصحيحين تفيد العلم النظري للإجماع على أن «الصحيحين مزية على غيرهما، وتلقت الأمة بقبولهما والإجماع قطعي، وهذا بهت فإن من رجع إلى وجدانه يعلم بالضرورة أن مجرد روايتهما لا يوجب اليقين البتة، وقد روي فيهما أخبار متناقضة، فلو أفادت روايتهما علماً لزم تحقق النقيضين في الواقع (وهذا) أي ما ذهب إليه ابن الصلاح وأتباعه بخلاف ما قاله الجمهور من الفقهاء والمحدثين، لأن انعقاد الإجماع على المزية على غيرهما من مرويات ثقات آخرین ممنوع والإجماع على مزيتهما على أنفسهما ما لا يفيد ولأن جلالة شأنهما وتلقي الأمة لكتابيهما والإجماع على المزية ولو سلم لا يستلزم ذلك القطع ،والعلم فإن القدر المسلم المتلقي بين الأمة ليس إلا أن رجال مروياتهما جامعة للشروط التي اشترطها الجمهور لقبول روايتهم، وهذا لا يفيد إلا الظن، وأما أن مروياتهما ثابتة عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم فلا إجماع عليه أصلاً، كيف ولا إجماع على صحة جميع ما في كتابيهما لأن رواتهما منهم قدريون وغيرهم من البدع، وقبول رواية أهل البدع مختلف فيه فأين الإجماع على صحة مرويات القدرية (غاية ما يلزم أن أحاديثهما أصح الصحيح يعني أنها مشتملة على الشروط عند الجمهور على الكمال، وهذا لا يفيد إلا الظن القوي، هذا هو الحق المتبع ، ولنعم ما قال الشيخ ابن الهمام إن قولهم بتقديم مروياتهما على مرويات الأئمة الآخرين قول لا يعتد به ولا يقتدى به، بل هو من تحكماتهم الصرفة، كيف لا وأن الأصحية من تلقاء عدالة الرواة وقوة ضبطهم وإذا كان رواة غيرهم عادلين ضابطين فهما وغيرهما على السواء ولا سبيل للحكم بميزتهما على غيرهما إلا تحكماً، والتحكم لا يلتفت إليه فافهم
Branch: Ibn al-Salah and a group of those labeled as Ahl al-Hadith claimed that the narrations of the two shaykhs, Muhammad ibn Isma'il al-Bukhari and Muslim ibn al-Hajjaj, the authors of the two Sahihs, produce theoretical knowledge (al-ilm al-nazari) due to the consensus that the two Sahihs have superiority over others, and the ummah received them with acceptance, and consensus is definitive. This is slander. For whoever refers to his inner sense knows by necessity that mere narration by them does not produce certainty at all. Contradictory reports have been narrated in them; if their narrations produced knowledge, then the realization of two contradictories in reality would be necessary. This, i.e., what Ibn al-Salah and his followers held, is contrary to what the majority of jurists and hadith scholars said. Because the occurrence of consensus on their superiority over the narrations of other trustworthy narrators is prevented, and consensus on their superiority over themselves is useless. And because their high status and the ummah's reception of their two books, and consensus on superiority, even if conceded, does not necessitate that certainty and knowledge. For the accepted amount received among the ummah is only that the narrators of their narrations are comprehensive of the conditions that the majority stipulated for accepting their narrations, and this only yields assumption (zann). As for that their narrations are established from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, there is no consensus on that at all. How (could there be) when among their narrators are Qadaris and other innovators, and the acceptance of the narrations of innovators is disputed. So where is the consensus on the authenticity of the narrations of the Qadaris? The most that follows is that their hadiths are the most authentic among authentic (asahh al-sahih), meaning that they include the conditions according to the majority completely, and this only yields strong assumption (al-zann al-qawi). This is the truth that must be followed. Excellent is what Shaykh Ibn al-Humam said: 'Their claim to give precedence to their narrations over the narrations of other imams is a statement that is not considered and not followed; rather it is from their pure arbitrariness. How could it be otherwise? For the greater authenticity stems from the justice and memory strength of narrators. If narrators of others are upright and precise, then they and others are equal, and there is no way to judge their superiority over others except by arbitrariness, and arbitrariness is not paid attention to. So understand.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage refutes Ibn al-Salah's claim that the narrations in Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim produce certain knowledge (ilm nazari) due to alleged consensus on their superiority and acceptance. The author argues that this is false: (a) Common sense shows mere narration does not produce certainty. (b) Contradictory hadiths in them would entail impossible contradictions. (c) There is no consensus on all narrations being sound; their narrators include Qadaris and innovators whose narrations are disputed. (d) The most that can be said is that their hadiths are the most authentic in terms of conditions, but that only gives strong zann, not certainty. The author supports Ibn al-Humam's view that superiority claims are arbitrary unless narrators are of equal reliability. This reflects the Hanafi position that khabar al-wahid, even from Bukhari, remains zanni.

Key Terms
العلم النظري (al-ilm al-nazari): Certain knowledge acquired through inference and evidence, as opposed to necessary knowledge
الأصحية (al-asahiyya): The quality of being more authentic; based on narrators' reliability and precision

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what do the narrations in Sahih al-Bukhari produce?
A) Certain knowledge (ilm qat'i)
B) Theoretical knowledge (ilm nazari)
C) Strong assumption (zann qawi)
D) Pure doubt (shakk)

Key Takeaway
No matter how authentic a hadith collection, its narrations remain zanni unless corroborated by tawatur or other certain proofs.

The Self-Referential Argument for Fabricated Hadith: A Usul Critique
Textual analysis of a logical proof for fabricated hadith with a chain-based critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi critique of a hadith used as dalil by identifying when a mu'allaq hadith invalidates a logical proof.

Core Idea
A mu'allaq hadith cannot ground a certain proof, even when the argument appears logically airtight.

This passage presents a clever logical argument that some attributed hadith are false, using the very hadith that warns against lying. But the author immediately questions the proof because the hadith is only found as mu'allaq. In Hanafi usul, a weak chain undermines certainty. Let's walk through the structure of this argument and its critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the argument's premises
State the two possibilities: either the hadith is authentic or it is not. If authentic, its content is true, so there is lying. If not, it is itself a lie, also proving the point. The conclusion: some hadith are fabricated.
This is a perfect logical syllogism (qiyas) – but usul requires more.
2
Recognize the rhetorical strength
The argument seems certain because it covers all possibilities. It appears to be a definitive proof (dalil qat'i) because the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises.
However, the certainty here is logical, not based on transmission.
3
Spot the weakness: mu'allaq chain
The author notes that this hadith is only found as mu'allaq – meaning the chain is missing at the beginning. In Hanafi usul, a mu'allaq hadith is not qat'i al-thubut (certain in transmission); it is zanni.
Even if the logic is sound, the proof relies on a zanni source.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
Because the hadith is mu'allaq, the inference is not valid for proving certainty (qat'). The conclusion may be plausible but not certain. The author says: 'even if conceded, it is not suitable for proving certainty.'
This highlights the Hanafi principle that the strength of a proof depends on both transmission (thubut) and meaning (dalala).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: بعض ما ينسب إلى الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم كذب عليه لقوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم سيكذب علي عزى إلى «جامع الأصول»، ووجه الاستدلال أن نسبة هذا الحديث إما صحيحة فيكون صادقاً قطعاً فلا بد من تحقق مصداقة الذي الكذب على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم قطعاً، وأما ليست صحيحة فهي هو كذب على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم فثبت المدعي على التقديرين، وعلى هذا لا أن الحديث إنما يوجد معلقاً، فلا یرد يصح الاستدلال، ولو سلم فلا يصلح لإثبات القطع والمقصود هذا وبقي فيه نوع مناقشة فإنه يختار الشق الأول ويقال : لا يدل إلا على
Issue: Some of what is attributed to the Messenger, peace be upon him and his family, is false (kadhib) upon him, due to his saying, peace be upon him and his family: 'There will be many liars against me,' attributed to Jami' al-Usul. The face of inference is that the attribution of this hadith either is correct, so it is absolutely truthful, so its content must be realized, which is certainly that there is lying upon the Messenger of Allah; or it is not correct, so it is itself a lie upon the Messenger of Allah. Thus the claim is proven in both cases. However, this is not the case because the hadith is only found as suspended (mu'allaq), so the inference is not valid. And even if conceded, it is not suitable for proving certainty, and the intent is this. There remains some discussion: he chooses the first alternative and says: it only indicates...
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This brief passage presents a logical argument that some hadith are fabricated. The proof uses the hadith itself: the Prophet said 'there will be many liars against me.' If this hadith is authentic, then it confirms that false hadith exist. If it is not authentic, then it is itself a false attribution, also proving the point. However, the author notes a problem: this hadith is only found mu'allaq (suspended in chain), so its authenticity is not established. Thus, the argument may not be sound for proving certainty. This is a typical Hanafi usul caution: one cannot use a deficient hadith to prove a principle.

Key Terms
معلق (mu'allaq): A hadith with a missing link in the chain at the beginning (from the compiler's side)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author question the validity of the proof that some hadith are fabricated using the hadith 'suyukdhabu 'alayya'?
A) Because the hadith is mutawatir and thus certain
B) Because the hadith is only found as mu'allaq (suspended), not fully authenticated
C) Because the hadith contradicts the Quran
D) Because the hadith is about a different topic

Key Takeaway
A mu'allaq hadith cannot ground a certain proof, even if the argument appears logically flawless.

Proof of False Hadith from Prophetic Statement and Reason
Establishing the existence of fabricated hadith via dual proof: the Prophet's own words and rational contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two proofs for the existence of fabricated hadith and apply the Hanafi principle of takhṣīṣ to reconcile apparently contradictory texts.

Core Idea
The existence of false hadith is proven both by the Prophet's own warning and by rational contradiction of certain narrations.

This passage from Ibn ʿAbd al-Shakūr's *Musallam al-Thubūt* with its commentary in *Fawātih al-Raḥamūt* establishes a fundamental justification for the science of hadith criticism. It provides two independent proofs: the Prophet's explicit prophecy that lies would be attributed to him, and the discovery of narrations that contradict reason. Through these, the need for rigorous authentication becomes self-evident.

Guided Path
1
State the two-fold proof
Recognize that the author presents two independent arguments for the existence of fabricated hadith: (1) the Prophet's own statement, and (2) the presence of hadith that contradict reason and cannot be interpreted away.
2
Analyze the disjunctive syllogism
Examine the first proof: the hadith 'Lies will be attributed to me' is either authentic or fabricated. If authentic, it predicts future lying, proving the phenomenon. If fabricated, it itself is a lie attributed to the Prophet, also proving fabrication. Both possibilities lead to the same conclusion.
This is a classic qiyās al-ʿaks (inference by contradiction).
3
Examine the rational contradiction example
Consider the second proof: the hadith 'No soul remains after 100 years' contradicts the observed reality of al-Khiḍr's continued existence. Since reason affirms al-Khiḍr's life (agreed by saints), the hadith must be false or require interpretation.
4
Discuss the rebuttal and takhṣīṣ
Note that the commentator in al-Ḥāshiyya objects: the hadith can be interpreted (taʾwīl) or its general term can be specified (takhṣīṣ) to exclude al-Khiḍr. This shows that apparent contradiction is not always decisive if interpretation is possible.
Hanafī usul: takhṣīṣ is common, so a text that can be specified is not qaṭʿī (definitive) in its generality.
5
Identify the ikhtilaf on al-Khiḍr's life
Understand that some scholars (like al-Bukhārī's hadith) use the '100 years' hadith to argue al-Khiḍr died, while the majority (including great awliyā') hold he is alive. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different interpretations of the same textual evidence.
The author sides with the majority, citing empirical evidence of saints meeting al-Khiḍr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: بعض ما ينسب إلى الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم كذب عليه لقوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم سيكذب علي عزى إلى «جامع الأصول»، ووجه الاستدلال أن نسبة هذا الحديث إما صحيحة فيكون صادقاً قطعاً فلا بد من تحقق مصداقة الذي الكذب على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم قطعاً، وأما ليست صحيحة فهي هو كذب على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم فثبت المدعي على التقديرين، وعلى هذا لا أن الحديث إنما يوجد معلقاً، فلا یرد يصح الاستدلال، ولو سلم فلا يصلح لإثبات القطع والمقصود هذا وبقي فيه نوع مناقشة فإنه يختار الشق الأول ويقال : لا يدل إلا على وقوع الكذب في المستقبل، ولا يلزم منه وقوعه إلى هذه الغاية، اللهم إلا أن يستدل بأن السين للاستقبال القريب ولأن منها ما يعارض العقل ولا يقبل التأويل وما يخالف العقل كاذب، فيستحيل صدوره عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم وقد يمثل له أي لما يخالف العقل برواية : لا يبقى على ظهر الأرض بعد مائة سنة نفس منفوسة) رواه الشيخان وغيرهما، فإنه قد بقي الأنفس الكثيرة، قال في الحاشية» : هذا غير مرضي، فإنه يقبل التأويل بأن المراد الموجودون الآن لا يتجاوزون المائة، بل لا تأويل على هذا فإن المشتق لما اتصف بالمبدإ في الحال، فالمنفوسة هي المنفوسة في الحال وقد يقرر الكلام بأن أبا العباس الخضر عليه السلام بقي، وكان نفساً منفوسة زمان التكلم ولا يذهب عليك أن التخصيص في العام ، غير عزيز، فليس مما لا يقبل التأويل، وربما يناقش بالتزام موت الخضر، بل اتخذه البعض مذهباً لهذا الحديث، ونقل عن البخاري رحمه الله تعالى فما وقع في حديث طويل حدث في خروج الدجال، فيخرج إليه رجل مؤمن فيقول : أنت الكذاب الذي حدثني به رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم فعند هؤلاء محمول على غير الخضر وأما الجمهور فعلى أن الخضر حي، وهو هذا الرجل المؤمن، وهو الحق، فإن أولياء الله قاطبة اتفقوا على أنه وقد لاقاه الأكثر، مثل قطب الأقطاب الشيخ محيي الدين عبد القادر الجيلاني الذي قدمه على رقاب كل ولي الله ، ومثل الشيخ الأكبر خاتم الولاية المحمدية الشيخ محيي الدین محمد بن العربي وغيرهما قدس الله أسرارهم
Issue: Some of what is attributed to the Messenger (peace be upon him, his family, and his Companions) is false upon him due to his saying (peace be upon him, his family, and his Companions): 'Lies will be attributed to me' [attributed to Jāmiʿ al-Uṣūl]. The face of inference: The attribution of this hadith is either correct, so he is truthful definitely, so the realization of its truthfulness necessitates the existence of lying upon the Messenger of God (peace be upon him and his Companions) definitely; or it is not correct, then it itself is a lie upon the Messenger of God, so the claim is established on both suppositions. Based on this, the hadith is only found suspended, so the inference is not invalid. Even if granted, it is not suitable for establishing certainty. The intended point is this. There remains in it a type of discussion: he chooses the first branch and says: it only indicates the occurrence of lying in the future, and it does not necessitate its occurrence up to now. However, it can be argued that the letter 'sīn' indicates near future, and because among them are those that contradict reason and do not accept interpretation, and whatever contradicts reason is false, so it is impossible for it to originate from the Messenger of God (peace be upon him and his family and Companions). An example of what contradicts reason is the narration: 'There will not remain on the surface of the earth after one hundred years any living soul' narrated by the two Shaykhs and others, for many souls have remained. He said in al-Ḥāshiyya: This is unsatisfactory, for it accepts interpretation: meaning those present at that time will not exceed one hundred. Rather there is no interpretation for this, because the derived word describes the one possessing the base meaning at the present time, so 'al-manfūsa' is the one born at the present time. But the discourse can be established that Abū al-ʿAbbās al-Khiḍr (peace be upon him) remained, and he was a living soul at the time of speech. And it should not escape you that specification (takhṣīṣ) of the general is not uncommon, so it is not something that does not accept interpretation. It may be challenged by affirming the death of al-Khiḍr; indeed some adopted this view due to this hadith. It is narrated from al-Bukhārī (may God have mercy on him) in a long hadith about the emergence of the Dajjāl: 'A believing man will go out to him and say: You are the liar about whom the Messenger of God (peace be upon him and his family and Companions) told me.' According to these, it is interpreted to refer to other than al-Khiḍr. As for the majority, they hold that al-Khiḍr is alive, and he is this believing man, and that is the truth. For the saints of God all agreed that he is alive, and most have met him, such as the Pole of Poles Shaykh Muḥyī al-Dīn ʿAbd al-Qādir al-Jīlānī who is placed above every saint of God, and such as the Greatest Shaykh, the Seal of Muḥammadan Sainthood, Shaykh Muḥyī al-Dīn Muḥammad ibn ʿArabī, and others, may God sanctify their secrets.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage addresses how we know that some hadith are fabricated. The author presents a two-fold proof: First, the Prophet's own statement 'Lies will be attributed to me' is either authentic (proving future false attribution) or inauthentic (itself a lie, thus proving the phenomenon). Second, there are hadith that contradict reason and cannot be interpreted allegorically, such as the claim that no living soul would remain after 100 years – yet figures like al-Khiḍr are believed to be alive. However, a note from al-Ḥāshiyya suggests that this hadith can be interpreted (e.g., as referring to people alive at that precise moment), and that takhṣīṣ of the general is common. The author then presents the debate about al-Khiḍr's survival, with the majority (including great saints) affirming his life. Ultimately, the existence of clearly false hadith is established, which supports the need for rigorous criticism of narrations.

Key Terms
تخصيص العام (takhṣīṣ al-ʿāmm): Limiting a general term (ʿāmm) to apply only to some of its individuals, based on evidence.
ما لا يقبل التأويل (mā lā yaqbalu al-taʾwīl): A text that cannot be reinterpreted away from its apparent meaning; usually because it leads to an impossible conclusion.
الاستدلال (al-istidlāl): Inference or derivation of a ruling or conclusion from evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the role of the harf 'sīn' in the hadith 'سيكذب علي'?
A) It indicates the past tense.
B) It indicates the near future.
C) It indicates a command.
D) It negates the action.

Key Takeaway
The existence of fabricated hadith is proven both deductively through the Prophet's own words and inductively through hadith that conflict with reason, necessitating the discipline of hadith criticism and the use of interpretive tools like takhṣīṣ.
