Causes of Fabricated Hadith: Forgetfulness, Mistake, and Desire
Explaining the causes of hadith fabrication: forgetfulness, honest mistake, and deliberate lying for worldly gain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three causes of fabricated hadith as expounded in Hanafi usul and apply them to evaluate narrator reliability.

Core Idea
Fabricated hadith arise from forgetfulness, honest mistakes, and deliberate lies—piety does not prevent error.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut details the causes that lead to false narrations attributed to the Prophet (peace be upon him). Understanding these causes is essential for hadith criticism (jarḥ wa taʿdīl) and for appreciating the rigorous methodology of the Hanafi school in preserving the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three causes
In the text, find the three causes of kadhb (fabrication) on the Prophet: nisyan (forgetfulness), ghalat (mistake), and ittiba' al-hawa (following desire).
2
Analyze the example for forgetfulness
Consider the story of Thabit ibn Musa: he heard a teacher praise him and thought it was part of the hadith chain. This shows how a righteous person can unintentionally spread a false report.
3
Analyze the example for mistake
Ibn Lahīʿa's books burned, so he narrated from memory and fell into error. This illustrates that even a previously trustworthy narrator can become unreliable if his primary source (written records) is lost.
4
Analyze the example for following desire
Contrast with deliberate fabricators like 'Abd al-Karim al-Wadda' and Ghiyath ibn Ibrahim, who lied for worldly gain. This shows the spectrum from unintentional to intentional falsehood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وسببه) أي سبب الكذب على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم نسيان الراوي) فيحفظ مكان حديث حديثاً آخر (أو غلطه) فيظن غير الحديث حديثاً، وذلك قد يكون لغلبة الصلاح والزهد والاشتغال بالعبادة، بحيث لم يتفرغ لضبط الحديث، وذلك كما حكي عن ثابت بن موسى الزاهد دخل على شريك القاضي والمستملي بين يديه وشريك يقول : حدثنا الأعمش عن سفيان عن جابر قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم ولم يذكر متن الحديث، ونظر إلى ثابت بن موسى فقال : من كثرت صلاته بالليل حسن وجهه بالنهار وأدبه مدح ثابت فظن ثابت أنه روى الحديث بالإسناد المذكور، فكان ثابت يرويه عن شريك، ومن الغلط والنسيان روايات ابن لهيعة وكان قد احترقت كتبه بمصر، فذهب حديثه، فكان يحدث عن حفظه فيروي المناكير، فصار ممن يحتج به، وقال الإمام أحمد بن جنبل سماع ابن المبارك وأقرانه الذين سمعوا منه قبل وفاته صحيح لاحتراق الكتب بعده أو اتباع الهوى فيضع الأحاديث ويكذب على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم قال ابن عدي: لما أخذ عبد الكريم الوضاع لتضرب عنقه قال : لقد وضعت فيكم أربعة آلاف حديث أحرم فيها وأحلل كذا في شرح النخبة»، كذا في «الحاشية»، ومن اتباع الهوى وضع الرجال الأحاديث للتقرب إلى الملوك مثل غياث بن إبراهيم : دخل على المهدي بن المنصور وكان يعجبه اللعب بالحمام فروى وقال : لا سبق إلا في خف أو حافر أو جناح فأمر له بعشرة آلاف درهم فلما قام ليخرج قال المهدي : أشهد أن قفاك قفا كذاب على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم، ما قال بعشرين سنة
(And its cause) i.e., the cause of lying upon the Messenger of God (peace be upon him and his family and Companions) is the forgetfulness of the narrator, so he memorizes in place of a hadith another hadith (or his mistake) so he thinks non-hadith is hadith. And that may be due to predominance of righteousness and asceticism and occupation with worship, such that he did not have leisure for preserving hadith. This is as narrated about Thābit ibn Mūsā al-Zāhid: he entered upon Sharīk al-Qāḍī while the scribe was in front of him and Sharīk was saying: 'We were told by al-Aʿmash from Sufyān from Jābir that he said: The Messenger of God (peace be upon him and his family and Companions) said...' and he did not mention the text of the hadith, and he looked at Thābit ibn Mūsā and said: 'Whoever increases his prayer at night, his face becomes beautiful by day' – he praised Thābit, so Thābit thought he had narrated the hadith with that chain. So Thābit used to narrate it from Sharīk. And from mistake and forgetfulness are the narrations of Ibn Lahīʿa: his books were burned in Egypt, so his hadith was lost, so he used to narrate from memory and thus narrated objectionable (munkar) reports, so he became one who is not relied upon. Imam Aḥmad ibn Ḥanbal said: The hearing of Ibn al-Mubārak and his peers who heard from him before his death is correct because the books burned afterwards. Or following desire, so he fabricates hadith and lies upon the Messenger of God (peace be upon him and his family and Companions). Ibn ʿAdī said: When ʿAbd al-Karīm al-Waḍḍāʿ was taken to be executed, he said: 'I have fabricated among you four thousand hadith, making lawful and unlawful therein' – so it is in Sharḥ al-Nukhba, etc. in al-Ḥāshiyya. And from following desire is the fabrications of men to gain closeness to kings, such as Ghiyāth ibn Ibrāhīm: he entered upon al-Mahdī ibn al-Manṣūr – he liked playing with pigeons – so he narrated and said: 'There is no racing except in a hoof, a foot, or a wing.' So he ordered ten thousand dirhams for him. When he stood to leave, al-Mahdī said: 'I testify that the back of your neck is the back of a liar upon the Messenger of God; what he said [is not true].'
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage details the three causes of false hadith. The first is nisyān (forgetfulness): a narrator like Thābit ibn Mūsā mistakenly thought a comment by a teacher was part of the hadith. The second is ghalat (mistake): Ibn Lahīʿa's books burned, so he narrated from memory and fell into error. The third is ittibāʿ al-hawā (following desire): deliberate fabrication for worldly motives, such as ʿAbd al-Karīm al-Waḍḍāʿ who admitted fabricating thousands of hadith, and Ghiyāth ibn Ibrāhīm who fabricated a hadith to please the caliph al-Mahdī. These examples illustrate the need for rigorous verification (jarḥ wa taʿdīl) in hadith criticism. The Hanafī usul tradition emphasizes that even righteous narrators can err, and that desire for status or money can lead to outright lies, reinforcing that only careful scrutiny of chains and content can protect the Sunnah.

Key Terms
نسيان (nisyān): Forgetfulness that causes a narrator to confuse one hadith with another or mistake a non-hadith statement for a hadith.
غلط (ghalaṭ): A mistake, such as narrating from memory after loss of written records, leading to inaccuracies.
اتباع الهوى (ittibāʿ al-hawā): Following personal desires, leading to deliberate fabrication of hadith for worldly gain or to promote sectarian views.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the reason for Ibn Lahīʿa's unreliable narrations?
A) He was a known liar.
B) His books were burned.
C) He was too pious to memorize.
D) He fabricated hadith for money.

Key Takeaway
Hadith fabrication can result from both unintentional errors (forgetfulness, mistake) and deliberate lying; rigorous criticism must account for all three causes.

Prohibition of Narrating Fabricated Hadith (al-Mawdu‘)
Discussion on the ruling of lying about the Prophet and narrating fabricated hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on narrating fabricated hadith and apply the Hanafi principle that narrating a mawdu‘ hadith is forbidden unless its status is clarified.

Core Idea
Intentional lying about the Prophet is a major sin; narrating a fabricated hadith is forbidden unless the narrator discloses its fabricated nature.

This lesson examines a critical usul discussion: the ruling on fabricated hadith (al-mawdu‘). The passage clarifies that it is forbidden to narrate such hadith even for good intentions, and establishes the obligation to disclose fabrication when narrating. The primary evidence is the hadith warning against lying, and the consensus on prohibition except with clarification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
Read the passage and extract the ruling on lying about the Prophet and narrating fabricated hadith. Note the different aspects: the act of lying itself (a major sin) and the act of narrating a fabricated hadith.
The ruling is explicit: lying is from the major sins, and narrating fabricated hadith is prohibited unless clarified.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the hadith cited as evidence: 'Whoever lies about me intentionally, let him take his seat in the Fire.' Determine its thubut (transmission) and dalala (meaning).
The hadith is from Muslim, ahad but considered qat'i in this context due to multiple transmissions.
3
Analyze the dalil's wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith proves the prohibition: the explicit threat of punishment indicates the severity and prohibited nature.
4
Identify the refuted opinion
Note the opinion attributed to some pseudo-Sufis that fabrication is permissible for encouragement and deterrence. Understand why this opinion is rejected.
The refutation points out that lying is a major sin, and the end does not justify the means.
5
Apply the ruling to the practice of narrating hadith
Based on the passage, state the condition under which a fabricated hadith may be narrated: only when the narrator clarifies it is fabricated. Otherwise, it is forbidden.
This is an example of a general rule with an exception.

Source Text

الأصل الثاني/ السنة سعيهم، ١٥٣ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم ،جناح، ولكن هذا أراد ليتقرب إلينا، يا غلام اذبح الحمام فقيل : ما ذنب الحمام؟ قال: من أجلهن كذب على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (وفي نخبة الفكر) روي عن بعض الكرامية والمتصوفة) وهم الذين أظهروا الصوفية بالتكلف وهم ليسوا من الصوفية في شيء بل هم يتشبهون بهم وقلوبهم قلوب الملاحدة (إباحة الوضع في الترغيب والترهيب ليرغب الرجال في الحسنات فيعملوا بها ويرهبوا عن السيئات فيجتنبوا عنها، وأما الصوفية حقاً فهم خيار الأمة براء عن مثل هذا التصنع كيف وهم لا يجوزون الافتراء على أحد، وإن كان الموضع موضع ترخص، وجل ، ، الأخذ بالعزائم وهم في الأكثر يستعملون نصح أنفسهم ولا ينصحون غيرهم نصائح حق إلا بعد تهذيبهم أنفسهم، فكيف يجترؤون على إهلاك أنفسهم بالكذب على سيد البشر صلوات الله عليه وآله وأصحابه لنصيحة باطلة قبيحة وذلك الوضع كما وقع عن أبي عصمة نوح ابن أبي مريم أنه وضع أحاديث في فضائل القرآن سورة سورة بعنوان : إن من قرأ سورة كذا فله كذا، وروي عن عكرمة عن ابن عباس وتارة يروى عن أبي بن كعب وهي الأحاديث التي نقلت تفسير البيضاوي عند ختم كل سورة فلما سئل من أين هذه الأحاديث؟ قال : لما رأيت اشتغال الناس بفقه أبي حنيفة ومغازي محمد بن إسحاق وأعرضوا عن حفظ القرآن وضعت هذه الأحاديث حسبة الله تعالى (وهو) أي هذا الرأي (خطأ) باطل (لأن تعمد الكذب) خصوصاً على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (من الكبائر) بل من أشدها، قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم : من كذب علي معتمداً فليتبوأ مقعده من النار» (واتفقوا على تحريم رواية الموضوع من الحديث : وهو الذي يكون في إسناده كاذب إلا ببيانه لقوله عليه) وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام): من حدّث عني بحديث يرى على البناء للمفعول أي يظن أنه كذب فهو أحد الكاذبين) رواه مسلم .
The second source / The Sunnah. Their endeavor. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Wing, but he wanted to draw close to us. O boy, slaughter the pigeons.' It was said: 'What is the sin of the pigeons?' He said: 'Because of them, lies were told about the Messenger of Allah.' (And in Nukhbat al-Fikr) It is narrated from some Karramiyya and pseudo-Sufis who show Sufism with affectation and are not from the Sufis at all, but rather imitate them while their hearts are hearts of atheists. They permit fabrication in exhortation and deterrence to make men desire good deeds and perform them, and to deter them from evil deeds so they avoid them. As for the true Sufis, they are the best of the community, innocent of such artifice. How could they allow lying against anyone, even if the context is one of leniency? They mostly adhere to firm resolutions and use sincere advice for themselves and do not advise others except after purifying themselves. So how would they dare to destroy themselves by lying about the master of mankind (peace be upon him) for a false and ugly advice? That fabrication occurred as from Abu 'Isma Nuh ibn Abi Maryam who fabricated hadith about the virtues of the Quran, surah by surah, with the title: 'Whoever recites such-and-such surah will have such-and-such.' It is narrated from 'Ikrimah from Ibn 'Abbas, and sometimes from Ubayy ibn Ka'b. These are the hadith that are quoted in the Tafsir of al-Baydawi at the end of each surah. When asked where these hadith came from, he said: 'When I saw people occupied with the fiqh of Abu Hanifah and the Maghazi of Muhammad ibn Ishaq, and they turned away from memorizing the Quran, I fabricated these hadith as a good deed for Allah.' (And this opinion) is false and invalid, because intentionally lying, especially about the Messenger of Allah, is among the major sins, indeed one of the most severe. The Prophet said: 'Whoever lies about me intentionally, let him take his seat in the Fire.' And they agreed on the prohibition of narrating fabricated hadith (i.e. that in whose chain there is a liar) except with clarification, because of his saying: 'Whoever relates a hadith from me that he thinks is a lie is one of the liars.' (Reported by Muslim).
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the second source of Islamic law: the Sunnah. It begins with a story about a boy who slaughtered pigeons and a false attribution to the Prophet. The author then discusses the permissibility of fabricating hadith for good purposes, attributed to some pseudo-Sufis, and refutes it strongly. He mentions the example of Abu 'Isma Nuh ibn Abi Maryam who fabricated hadith on the virtues of Quranic surahs. The ruling is clear: intentionally lying about the Prophet is a major sin, and fabricating hadith is forbidden. Furthermore, it is prohibited to narrate a fabricated hadith unless one clarifies its status. The hadith 'Whoever relates a hadith from me that he thinks is a lie is one of the liars' (Muslim) is cited as evidence.

Key Terms
الموضوع (al-mawdu'): A fabricated hadith, one whose chain contains a liar who invented the text.
الكذب على رسول الله (al-kadhib 'ala Rasul Allah): Lying about the Prophet, which includes fabricating hadith or falsely attributing statements to him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Narrating a fabricated hadith is prohibited; only when the narrator clarifies its fabricated status does it become permissible.

The Prophetic Silence: Zann not Qat' — A Usul Principle
Analysis of prophetic silence as evidence in usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that the Prophet's silence on a report gives presumption (zann) not certainty (qat'), and identify the five possibilities that prevent certainty.

Core Idea
Prophet's silence yields strong presumption, not certainty, due to possible reasons for silence.

This lesson examines a key usul al-fiqh principle: the legal weight of the Prophet's silence when a report is narrated in his presence. The Hanafi school holds that such silence creates a presumption (zann) of truth, but not certainty (qat'), because several possibilities may explain the silence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for deriving rulings from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
The author states that if a report is narrated in the Prophet's presence and he does not deny it, the apparent (zahir) is truthfulness, giving a presumption.
2
Distinguish zann from qat'
Recognize that this is only presumption, not certainty. Why? Because of possible reasons for silence.
3
Enumerate the possibilities
List the five possibilities given: the Prophet may not have heard, not understood, considered it worldly, postponed denial, or denial would be futile due to obstinacy.
These are the only possibilities considered in the text.
4
Refute the minor sin argument
Some suggested lack of certainty because the Prophet might have refrained from denying as a minor sin. The author refutes this by asserting prophetic immunity from even minor sins.
This refutation is key to the Hanafi position.
5
Apply the principle
In practical usul, this means that reports from the prophetic era that were not denied are strong evidence but not absolutely certain. They can be used for rulings but are subject to further analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: إذا أخبر بحضرته عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام فلم ينكر ذلك الخبر (فالظاهر) المظنون (الصدق أي صدق الخبر، لأن الظاهر تقرير ذلك الخبر (لا القطع) بصدقه (كما ظن لاحتمال أنه ما سمع) الخبر (أو ما فهم) وما علم صدقه ولا كذبه لكونه دنيوياً (أو رأى تأخير الإنكار ) إلى وقت الحاجة (أو) رأى (عدم إفادته) أي إفادة الإنكار لكون المخبر متعنتاً، ومع جواز هذه الاحتمالات لا قطع وما قيل إنه لا قطع لجواز ارتكابه عدم الاخبار لكونه صغيرة وهي جائزة على الأنبياء فرده المصنف بقوله : (وأما تجويزه صغيرة فبعيد جداً، فإنه خلاف العبادة قطعاً، بل لا يكاد يصح فإن المصنف قد بين سابقاً عدم صدور الصغيرة عن الأنبياء وبراءة شأنهم عنه قطعاً كخلاف (العادة كما أن تجويز السكوت على خلاف العادة بعيد جماعة
Issue: If a report is narrated in his presence (the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him) and he does not deny that report, then the apparent (i.e. the presumed) is truthfulness of the report, because the apparent (zahir) is that the report is confirmed. But there is no certainty of its truthfulness, as some think, because of the possibility that he did not hear the report, or did not understand, and did not know its truth or falsehood because it was a worldly matter, or he saw postponing the denial until needed, or he saw that denial would not be beneficial because the informant is obstinate. Given these possibilities, there is no certainty. And as for what is said that there is no certainty because it is possible he refrained from reporting it (the denial) because it is a minor sin, and minor sins are permissible for prophets, the author refuted this by saying: 'Allowing a minor sin is extremely unlikely, because it is contrary to devotion (ibadah) certainly, rather it is almost impossible, for the author has previously explained that minor sins do not occur from prophets, and they are absolutely innocent of that, as the custom.' And allowing silence contrary to custom is extremely unlikely.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage deals with the legal weight of a report narrated in the presence of the Prophet (peace be upon him) without his denial. The author states that such silence gives a presumption (zann) of truth, not certainty (qat'), because there are various possibilities for his silence: he may not have heard, not understood, considered it a worldly matter, postponed denial, or saw that denial would be futile. Some argued that lack of certainty could also be because the Prophet might have avoided denying as a minor sin, but the author refutes this, stating that prophets are immune from even minor sins, as established earlier. Thus, the Prophet’s silence does not produce certainty, only a strong presumption.

Key Terms
الظن (al-zann): Presumption, the weight of evidence leaning towards truth but not reaching certainty.
القطع (al-qat'): Certainty, conclusive knowledge that admits no doubt.
السكوت (al-sukut): Silence, specifically the Prophet's silence when a report is narrated in his presence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling regarding a report narrated in the presence of the Prophet without his denial?
A) It is certain to be true.
B) It gives a presumption of truth but not certainty.
C) It is automatically rejected.
D) It is considered false.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the Prophet's silence on a report yields strong presumption (zann) but not certainty (qat'), due to several plausible reasons for silence.

Tawatur Sukuti: Certainty from Collective Silence
Definition and evidence for tawatur sukuti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tawatur sukuti, explain how collective silence yields certainty by customary experience, and apply the concept to the classical example of Umar's statement about Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
The silence of a large informed group about a report confirms its truth by customary experience.

In Hanafi usul, certainty (qat') can be established not only through direct verbal transmission but also through the collective silence of a knowledgeable group. This lesson unpacks the concept of tawatur sukuti, where silence in response to a report is taken as a powerful indicator of its truth. We will explore the rationale based on customary experience and see it applied in the choice of Abu Bakr as caliph.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
Read the passage and determine what the author is asserting about the legal effect of collective silence on a report.
The claim is that silence yields certainty (qat'), not merely presumption (zann).
2
Understand the Rationale
Examine why silence of a large group is considered proof. The author cites customary experience ('adah): it is extremely improbable that a large number would remain silent if the report were false, especially when they have no motive to suppress denial.
‘Adah is a valid source of knowledge in Hanafi usul when it is consistent and reliable.
3
Analyze the Example
Consider Umar's statement about Abu Bakr: 'The Messenger of Allah put you forward in our religious affairs...' made in front of a large gathering. Their silence indicated the truth of the statement, providing certainty that the Prophet had indeed given Abu Bakr precedence.
The Companions' silence is not passive; it is an active confirmation given the context and their stakes in the matter.
4
Distinguish from Other Forms
Contrast tawatur sukuti with verbal tawatur (where many narrators explicitly transmit) and with mere tacit approval that may only yield zann under certain conditions.
The key difference is the number and the context: tawatur sukuti requires a large group and circumstances that preclude collusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: (إذا أخبر بحضرة خلق كثيراً فأمسكوا عن تكذيبه يفيد ظن صدقه لأن سكوت عن استكشاف ما يحتمل الكذب عندهم بعيد غاية البعد وإن لم يكن خبر غريب) أخبر به بل يكون بحيث لو كان لعلمه الجماعة (ولا) (حامل لهم على السكوت من موانع الإنكار، بل يظهر بقرائن الحال أن سكوتهم لصدق الخبر عندهم (فيفيد القطع) بصدق الخبر (بالعادة) فإن العادة تحيل كذب هذا الخبر وهذا ظاهر جداً (وهذا تواتر سكوتي) مثاله ما قال أمير المؤمنين عمر حين بايع أمير المؤمنين الصديق الأكبر : قدّمك رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم في أمر ديننا فمن يؤخرك في أمر دنيانا؟ بحضرة جم غفير قد شاركوه في سبب العلم وكان اجتماعهم لتعيين الخليفة وأحوالهم كانت شاهدة بأنه لو كان فيه نحو من الريبة لما سكتوا فأفاد القطع بأنه قدّمه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم في أمر ديني، لكن من لم يجعل الله له نوراً فما له من نور .
Issue: If a report is narrated in the presence of a large number of people, and they refrain from denying it, it yields a presumption of its truth, because their silence about investigating what is likely to be a lie is extremely improbable. Even if the report is not strange, but rather such that if it were false, the group would have known it, and there is no motive for them to remain silent due to obstacles to denial. Rather, it appears from the circumstances that their silence is due to the truth of the report in their view. So it yields certainty of the truth of the report by customary experience, for custom precludes the falsity of such a report. This is evident. And this is called 'tawatur sukuti' (silent tawatur). An example is what the Commander of the Faithful Umar said when he gave allegiance to the Commander of the Faithful, the Greater Truthful (Abu Bakr): 'The Messenger of Allah put you forward in our religious affairs, so who can put you back in our worldly affairs?' in the presence of a large multitude who shared in the cause of knowledge. Their gathering was to appoint the caliph, and their circumstances witnessed that if there had been any suspicion, they would not have remained silent. So it yielded certainty that the Prophet had put him forward in a religious matter. But he whom Allah has not granted light has no light.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage discusses 'tawatur sukuti' (silent tawatur), a concept in hadith criticism. When a report is narrated in the presence of a large group of people who are capable of knowing its truth, and they all remain silent and do not deny it, this silence indicates that the report is true. The reason is that it is extremely unlikely that such a large group would remain silent if the report were false, especially when there is no motive for them to stay silent. Such silence, based on customary experience ('adah), produces certainty (qat') about the truth of the report. The author gives the example of Umar's statement about Abu Bakr, which was made in the presence of a large gathering during the selection of the caliph. The silence of the Companions confirmed that the Prophet had indeed designated Abu Bakr in religious matters, thus producing certainty.

Key Terms
التواتر السكوتي (al-tawatur al-sukuti): A type of tawatur where a report is narrated in the presence of many people and they remain silent, indicating its truth.
العادة (al-'adah): Customary experience or habitual practice that provides epistemic certainty.
القطع (al-qat'): Certainty, the highest level of epistemic conviction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the example given in the text for tawatur sukuti?
A) The hadith about ablution
B) Umar's statement about Abu Bakr being put forward by the Prophet
C) The story of the boy and the pigeons
D) The hadith about intentions

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi usul, the silence of a large, knowledgeable group with no motive for concealment yields certainty of the report's truth through customary experience.

Refutation of Scholarly Acceptance Argument
Refutation of the claim that scholarly interaction equals consensus on acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in the argument that scholarly engagement with a report constitutes consensus on its acceptance.

Core Idea
Interpretation (ta'wil) may be a concession, not acceptance, so it does not prove consensus.

This passage addresses a common argument that if scholars interact with a hadith—some using it as proof and others interpreting it—then there is consensus on its acceptance. But the author shows why this reasoning is weak by explaining that interpretation may be a concession (tanzul) rather than genuine acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
Recognize that the argument asserts that a report narrated in the presence of scholars, which is either used as evidence or interpreted, is considered certain because scholarly engagement implies acceptance.
The claim is that using a report as evidence (ihtijaj) or interpreting it (ta'wil) are both forms of acceptance (qabul).
2
Understand the Supposed Proof
The argument reasons that if scholars did not accept the report, they would have rejected it outright. Since they did not reject it (some used it, others interpreted it), this indicates consensus (ijma') on acceptance.
The proof relies on the assumption that interpretation is a form of acceptance.
3
Examine the Flaw
The author points out that interpretation may be a concession (tanzul), meaning a scholar interprets the report not because they accept its authenticity, but to harmonize it with their own views or to avoid open rejection. Thus, interpretation does not prove acceptance.
Tanzul is a key concept: the interpreter concedes for the sake of argument or reconciliation, not because they affirm the report's truth.
4
Conclusion
The argument is weak because it confuses two types of engagement: genuine acceptance and interpretive accommodation. Therefore, it does not establish certainty.
This refutation shows the limits of using scholarly interaction as proof of a hadith's authenticity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: قيل : من المقطوع خبر العلماء) أي الخبر الذي رواه أحد بحضرة العلماء (ما بين محتج به ومؤول (له أي احتج البعض به وأول الآخرون (لأنه إجماع على القبول لأن الاحتجاج قبول ،له وكذا التأويل وإلا أنكروه (وهو ضعيف) لأن التأويل يجوز أن يكون على التنزل . مسألة: (بعض الزيدية قالوا : بقاء النقل مع توفر الدواعي على إبطاله يدل على القطع وليس بشيء) لأن عدم تأثير الدواعي في بطلان الباطل لا يفيد ظن صحته فضلاً عن صحته،
Issue: It is said that among the certain matters is the report of scholars, i.e., a report narrated by someone in the presence of scholars, which is either used as evidence by some and interpreted by others, because this is a consensus on acceptance, since using as evidence is acceptance, and so is interpretation, otherwise they would have rejected it. But this is weak, because interpretation may be based on concession (tanzul). Issue: Some Zaydis said that the continuity of transmission despite the presence of motives to refute it indicates certainty, but it is nothing, because the lack of effect of the motives on invalidating falsehood does not yield a presumption of its truth, let alone certainty.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents two weak arguments for establishing certainty of a hadith. The first is that if a report is narrated in the presence of scholars and some use it as evidence while others interpret it in a compatible manner, this supposedly shows consensus on acceptance, which would make the report certain. The author dismisses this as weak because the interpretation might be a concession (tanzul) and not a genuine acceptance of the report's authenticity. The second argument, attributed to some Zaydis, is that if a report continues to be transmitted even though there are strong motives to refute it (if it were false), then this persistence indicates its truth. The author rejects this because falsehood might persist despite motives to refute it; the fact that motives do not destroy it does not prove its truth. This is a logical argument.

Key Terms
التأويل (al-ta'wil): Interpretation of a text in a way that reconciles it with one's position, not necessarily accepting its outward meaning.
التنزل (al-tanzul): Concession; an interpretive move that does not affirm the report's authenticity but harmonizes it with one's own views.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school consider the first argument (scholarly acceptance) weak?
A) Because scholars never engage with weak hadiths.
B) Because interpretation may be a concession (tanzul), not acceptance.
C) Because consensus requires explicit statements.
D) Because the report must be mutawatir to be certain.

Key Takeaway
Ta'wil does not equal acceptance; it may be a concession, so scholarly engagement does not imply consensus.

6 min
Refutation of Zaydi Continuity Argument
Refutation of the claim that continuity of transmission indicates certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Zaydi argument—that continuity of transmission despite motives to refute indicates certainty—is logically flawed.

Core Idea
The persistence of falsehood despite motives to refute it does not prove its truth.

This passage refutes a Zaydi argument that if a report continues to be transmitted even though there are strong motives to refute it (if it were false), then its survival indicates certainty. The author dismisses this as unsound because falsehood can persist despite opposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Zaydi Claim
Some Zaydis argue that if a report remains in transmission despite the presence of motives to challenge and refute it, then that persistence demonstrates its truth with certainty.
The argument is based on the idea that falsehood would have been exposed and eliminated by those who have motives to do so.
2
Examine the Logical Structure
The argument assumes that if the report were false, the motives would certainly lead to its refutation and disappearance. Since it continues, it must be true.
This is a form of argument from silence or from lack of refutation.
3
Identify the Flaw
The author points out that the absence of effect of the motives on invalidating falsehood does not even give a presumption (zann) of truth, let alone certainty. Falsehood can persist despite motives to refute it—for example, due to vested interests, social pressure, or lack of effective means.
The key point is that the continuity of transmission is not a reliable indicator of truth; falsehood can also be transmitted.
4
Conclusion
The Zaydi argument is rejected because it relies on an invalid inference: the persistence of a report, even with opposing motives, does not establish its authenticity.
This refutation reinforces the Hanafi principle that certainty requires more than mere survival of transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: قيل : من المقطوع خبر العلماء) أي الخبر الذي رواه أحد بحضرة العلماء (ما بين محتج به ومؤول (له أي احتج البعض به وأول الآخرون (لأنه إجماع على القبول لأن الاحتجاج قبول ،له وكذا التأويل وإلا أنكروه (وهو ضعيف) لأن التأويل يجوز أن يكون على التنزل . مسألة: (بعض الزيدية قالوا : بقاء النقل مع توفر الدواعي على إبطاله يدل على القطع وليس بشيء) لأن عدم تأثير الدواعي في بطلان الباطل لا يفيد ظن صحته فضلاً عن صحته،
Issue: It is said that among the certain matters is the report of scholars, i.e., a report narrated by someone in the presence of scholars, which is either used as evidence by some and interpreted by others, because this is a consensus on acceptance, since using as evidence is acceptance, and so is interpretation, otherwise they would have rejected it. But this is weak, because interpretation may be based on concession (tanzul). Issue: Some Zaydis said that the continuity of transmission despite the presence of motives to refute it indicates certainty, but it is nothing, because the lack of effect of the motives on invalidating falsehood does not yield a presumption of its truth, let alone certainty.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents two weak arguments for establishing certainty of a hadith. The first is that if a report is narrated in the presence of scholars and some use it as evidence while others interpret it in a compatible manner, this supposedly shows consensus on acceptance, which would make the report certain. The author dismisses this as weak because the interpretation might be a concession (tanzul) and not a genuine acceptance of the report's authenticity. The second argument, attributed to some Zaydis, is that if a report continues to be transmitted even though there are strong motives to refute it (if it were false), then this persistence indicates its truth. The author rejects this because falsehood might persist despite motives to refute it; the fact that motives do not destroy it does not prove its truth. This is a logical argument.

Key Terms
الدواعي (al-dawa'i): Strong motives or incentives to act, here referring to the desire of opponents to refute a false report.
الباطل (al-batil): Falsehood; something that is false or invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the Zaydi argument about continuity of transmission with motives to refute it considered weak?
A) Because motives do not always lead to action.
B) Because falsehood can persist despite motives to refute it.
C) Because continuity requires tawatur.
D) Because the transmission is not verified.

Key Takeaway
The persistence of falsehood despite motives to refute it does not prove its truth; continuity alone is not evidence of authenticity.

Silent Tawatur: Certainty Through Silence
Defining silent tawatur with an example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tawatur sukuti, explain its basis in custom, and apply it to a scenario using the Sahabah's silence as a proof of certainty.

Core Idea
Silence in a context where objection is expected yields certainty of truth.

In this lesson, we explore a distinctive Hanafi usul concept: silent tawatur (tawatur sukuti). Unlike verbal tawatur, this type of mutawatir report derives its strength from the meaningful silence of a large group that would have objected if the statement were false. The passage defines the concept, grounds it in the principle of custom ('adah), and illustrates it with a powerful example from the early Muslim community.

Guided Path
1
Define Tawatur Sukuti
Identify the key elements: a report, a multitude present, a context where denial is expected, and silence that indicates acceptance.
Contrast with verbal tawatur where the transmission is through explicit speech.
2
Understand the Role of Custom
Recognize that the certainty arises from the custom ('adah) that such silence would not occur if the report were false. Custom makes denial 'impossible' (muhal) in normal circumstances.
This is a form of implicit attestation.
3
Analyze the Example
Examine 'Umar's statement about Abu Bakr and the Sahabah's silence. Consider the gathering for the caliph appointment, the multitude present, and the natural expectation of objection if the claim were false.
The silence is circumstantial evidence (qarina) that proves the truth of the claim.
4
Draw the Epistemological Conclusion
Conclude that such silence yields certainty (qat') of the report's truth. This is a valid form of tawatur in Hanafi usul.
The final sentence warns that not everyone accepts this truth, but it is an objective proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإنكار، بل يظهر بقرائن الحال أن سكوتهم لصدق الخبر عندهم (فيفيد القطع) بصدق الخبر (بالعادة) فإن العادة تحيل كذب هذا الخبر وهذا ظاهر جداً (وهذا تواتر سكوتي) مثاله ما قال أمير المؤمنين عمر حين بايع أمير المؤمنين الصديق الأكبر : قدّمك رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم في أمر ديننا فمن يؤخرك في أمر دنيانا؟ بحضرة جم غفير قد شاركوه في سبب العلم وكان اجتماعهم لتعيين الخليفة وأحوالهم كانت شاهدة بأنه لو كان فيه نحو من الريبة لما سكتوا فأفاد القطع بأنه قدّمه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم في أمر ديني، لكن من لم يجعل الله له نوراً فما له من نور .
Denial, rather it appears from circumstantial evidence that their silence (indicates) the truthfulness of the report according to them (so it yields certainty) of the truth of the report (by custom) because custom deems the falsity of this report impossible and this is very apparent. (And this is silent tawatur). Its example is what the Commander of the Faithful Umar said when he pledged allegiance to the Commander of the Faithful, the Greater Truthful (Abu Bakr): 'The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and his family, and his Companions, advanced you in our religious affairs, so who can delay you in our worldly affairs?' In the presence of a great multitude who shared in the cause of knowledge, and their gathering was for the appointment of the caliph, and their circumstances were witness that if there had been any suspicion, they would not have remained silent. So it yielded certainty that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and his family, and his Companions, had advanced him in religious affairs. But for whom Allah has not appointed light, there is no light for him.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage defines 'silent tawatur', a key Hanafi usul concept. It describes how the silence of a large group (here the Sahabah) in response to a statement, given that they would have denied it if false, provides certainty (qat') of its truth. The author uses the example of 'Umar's declaration that the Prophet (peace be upon him) had advanced Abu Bakr in religious matters; no companion objected, so their silence confirms the truth. This is a form of mutawatir report (tawatur sukuti). The final sentence warns that only those whom Allah guides accept this truth.

Key Terms
تواتر سكوتي (tawātur sukūtī): A type of mutawatir report where the silence of a multitude indicates their agreement with a claim, yielding certainty.
العادة (al-ʿādah): Custom or normal human practice that renders certain outcomes inevitable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the example given in the passage for silent tawatur?
A) The silence of the Sahabah when Umar said the Prophet advanced Abu Bakr
B) The consensus of the Companions on the Quran
C) The narration of a solitary hadith
D) The practice of the people of Madinah

Key Takeaway
Silent tawatur, based on custom, is a valid Hanafi method for establishing certainty from the silence of a knowledgeable multitude in a context where objection is expected.

Do Scholars' Treatment of a Report Produce Certainty?
Evaluating the claim that a report used by some scholars and interpreted by others yields certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the claim that a report accepted by some scholars and interpreted by others yields certainty, by identifying the flaw in the reasoning based on the nature of ta'wil.

Core Idea
Interpretation by scholars may be a concession, not proof of acceptance—so no certainty.

This lesson examines a claim that a report presented before scholars, if some use it as evidence and others interpret it, becomes certain because that indicates consensus on acceptance. The Hanafi author rejects this, arguing that interpretation (ta'wil) may be a concession (tanazzul) rather than genuine acceptance. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the epistemological weight of reports.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
Locate the claim being refuted: 'It is said: among the certain is the report of the scholars, that which is used as evidence and interpreted.'
The claim rests on the idea that both using as proof and interpreting indicate acceptance.
2
Analyze the Reasoning Behind the Claim
Understand why someone would think this yields certainty: because using as evidence is acceptance, and interpretation also implies acceptance; otherwise they would have rejected it.
This assumes that interpretation is always a sign of confirming the report's authenticity.
3
Identify the Hanafi Rebuttal
The author states the claim is 'weak' because interpretation could be a concession (tanazzul), not genuine acceptance.
Tanazzul means the scholar accommodates the report without committing to its truth.
4
Distinguish Between Types of Ta'wil
Recognize that ta'wil may be either a legitimate interpretation (accepting the report but reading it metaphorically) or a concessionary interpretation to avoid outright rejection.
Only the former indicates acceptance; the latter is not evidence of certainty.
5
Apply the Principle
When analyzing a report's authority, mere absence of rejection or presence of interpretation does not establish certainty. One must examine whether the interpretation is genuine or a concession.
This prevents overestimating the strength of a report based on scholarly behavior alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: (قيل : من المقطوع خبر العلماء) أي الخبر الذي رواه أحد بحضرة العلماء (ما بين محتج به ومؤول (له أي احتج البعض به وأول الآخرون (لأنه إجماع على القبول لأن الاحتجاج قبول ،له وكذا التأويل وإلا أنكروه (وهو ضعيف) لأن التأويل يجوز أن يكون على التنزل .
Issue: It is said: Among the certain matters is the report of the scholars, i.e., the report that one narrated in the presence of scholars, (is considered between that which is used as evidence and that which is interpreted) i.e., some used it as evidence and others interpreted it (because it is consensus on acceptance, because using it as evidence is acceptance, and so is interpretation, otherwise they would have rejected it) (and it is weak) because interpretation could be a concession.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This short passage refutes a claim that a report presented before scholars, where some accept it as proof and others offer an interpretation (without outright rejection), yields certainty. The argument is that such a range of responses does not indicate consensus on acceptance; interpretation could be a form of accommodation (tanzul) rather than true acceptance. The author deems the claim weak, in line with Hanafi caution about deriving certainty from scholarly behavior alone.

Key Terms
محتج به (muḥtajj bihī): A report used as evidence (hujja) in deriving rulings.
مؤول له (mu'awwal lahū): A report that scholars interpret metaphorically or otherwise to avoid rejecting it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because scholars might interpret a report as a concession, not true acceptance
B) Because scholars cannot reach consensus on anything
C) Because the report is only about worldly matters
D) Because the report is not from the Prophet

Key Takeaway
Interpretation of a report by scholars does not automatically indicate acceptance; it may be a concession, so it does not produce certainty.

Refuting the Zaydi Argument: Survival Does Not Imply Certainty
Refuting the Zaydi argument that survival implies certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Zaydi claim that the survival of a transmission despite incentives to refute it indicates certainty, and apply the Hanafi critique using the concept of dawāʿī.

Core Idea
Survival under refutation does not prove truth; falsehoods also persist.

In usul al-fiqh, the debate over what constitutes certainty (qat') is fundamental. Some Zaydis argued that if a report or belief survives despite strong motives to disprove it, that survival itself grants certainty. The Hanafi school rejects this, explaining that persistence alone is not evidence of truth. This lesson examines that refutation and the critical lesson it teaches about evaluating evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Zaydi claim
The Zaydi claim is that the survival of a transmission despite motives to refute it indicates certainty (qat').
2
Understand the Hanafi counterargument
The Hanafis argue that survival does not even provide a presumption (zann), let alone certainty, because falsehoods can also survive refutation attempts.
3
Analyze the example of polytheistic beliefs
Polytheistic beliefs persisted despite strong refutation by Muslims; their survival did not make them true. This illustrates that survival is not evidence of truth.
The example shows that falsehoods can be long-lasting.
4
Extract the usul principle
Certainty requires positive evidence (dalil qat'i), not mere survival. The concept of dawāʿī (motives) is not a valid indicator of truth.
This is a key Hanafi principle in evaluating transmissions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: (بعض الزيدية قالوا : بقاء النقل مع توفر الدواعي على إبطاله يدل على القطع وليس بشيء) لأن عدم تأثير الدواعي في بطلان الباطل لا يفيد ظن صحته فضلاً عن القطع، كيف بل ربما كان ضد الشيء مقطوعاً من توفر الدواعي على بطلان ذلك الشيء، ولا يبطل كعقائد المشركين .
Issue: Some Zaydis said: The survival of a transmission despite the presence of motives to refute it indicates certainty, and it is nothing. Because the lack of effect of motives in eliminating falsehood does not indicate the assumption of its correctness, let alone certainty. How? Rather, sometimes the opposite of something is certain from the presence of motives to refute that thing, and it does not become void, like the beliefs of the polytheists.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage refutes a Zaydi argument that if a report or belief survives despite strong incentives to disprove it, that survival itself gives certainty of its truth. The author argues that falsehoods can persist even with many attempts to refute them; for example, polytheistic beliefs were refuted by Muslims but persisted. Therefore, survival does not even give a presumption of truth (zann), let alone certainty (qat'). This is a Hanafi rejection of using 'survival under opposition' as a proof of authenticity, emphasizing instead the need for positive evidence.

Key Terms
الدواعي (ad-dawāʿī): Motives or incentives that would normally lead to refutation or suppression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example does the text give to refute the Zaydi claim about survival indicating truth?
A) Beliefs of polytheists
B) Silence of the Sahabah
C) The Quran's preservation
D) The hadith of Ghadir

Key Takeaway
The survival of a transmission despite incentives to refute it is not a valid indicator of its truth; falsehoods can also persist.

Tawaffur al-Dawa'i: Certainty of Falsity in Solitary Reports
Usul rule on solitary reports when mass transmission expected

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of 'tawaffur al-dawa'i' to identify when a solitary report is certainly false (maqtu' bi-kidhbihi), and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shi'i stance on Ghadir Khumm.

Core Idea
A solitary report of an event that should be widely known is certainly false – custom deems concealment impossible.

In Hanafi usul, the credibility of a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) depends on whether the event would have naturally motivated widespread transmission (tawaffur al-dawa'i). This lesson examines the principle that when such motives exist, a single narrator's report is deemed certainly false. The passage applies this to the Ghadir Khumm incident, challenging the Shi'i claim of a public appointment of Ali. We will unpack the derivation, the role of 'adah (custom), and the response to concerns about accusing Companions of lying.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine the situation: one narrator reports an event that, if true, would have been witnessed by many others who would have strong motives to transmit it. Here, the event is the alleged appointment of Ali at Ghadir Khumm in front of over 100,000 people.
The key is the presence of 'tawaffur al-dawa'i' – motives so strong that silence would be impossible.
2
Apply the principle of 'adah
Custom ('adah) judges that such an event cannot be concealed; therefore the solitary report is certainly false (yuqta'u bi-kidhbihi). This is a rational (aqli) proof, not based on scripture.
The text states: 'العادة قاضية به أي بالقطع بالكذب' – custom decrees the judgment.
3
Handle the objection regarding Companions
If accepting this principle would imply that a Companion lied, the Hanafi response is that such a scenario never actually occurred from Companions; any apparent solitary report from a Companion should be attributed to forgetfulness or error, not falsehood.
The justice of the majority of Companions is certain (qat'i) based on Quran and Sunnah.
4
Contrast with the Shi'i position
The Shi'a claim that the Ghadir event is true and that the multitude concealed it. The Hanafi response: if concealment were possible, it would undermine the reliability of all transmission, even the Quran. Thus, their claim is self-defeating.
The Shi'i reliance on solitary reports (khabar al-wahid) for such an important event is inconsistent with their demand for certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: إذا انفرد واحد بما تتوفر الدواعي إليه أي إذا انفرد واحد بما تتوفر الدواعي إلى نقله لو كان (وفي سبب العلم شاركه خلق كثير) لو كان لكونهم مشاهدين يقطع بكذبه) وحاصل المسألة رواية الفرد خبراً لو كان لعلم خلق كثير ذلك الخبر، ولم يروه من ذلك الخلق أحد أصلاً، أو روى واحد ولم يروه من سواه يقطع بكذب هذا الخبر، لا سيما إذا ادعى المخبر مشاركة الكل أو الأكثر في العلم به، فإن قلت، يلزم كذب الصحابي والعياذ بالله، لأن كذب الخبر يستلزم كذب المخبر، قلت لزوم هذا الأمر الفظيع إنما يكون لو وقوع من الصحابة الإخبار بهذا النمط، وهو ممنوع وعليك بالاستقراء، وأجاب المصنف بأنه يحمل على السهو والنسيان والغلط، وبالجملة على العذر الصحيح إن كان إلا فيلتزم كذب المخبر والحكم بعدالة الصحابي مظنون معتبر ما لم يوجد دليل العدم والحق إسقاط قوله الأخير من البين والاكتفاء بالحمل على السهو والشبهة فإن عدالة الأكثرين قطعية، كيف وقد شهد الله تعالى بعدالة أصحاب بيعة الرضوان رضي الله تعالى عنهم ورسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم بعدالة من لا يحصى كما لا يخفى خلافاً للشيعة الشنيعة زاعمين النص الجلي على إمامة) أمير المؤمنين (علي) بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم؛ قالوا إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أعطاه الخلافة في غدير خم حين المراجعة من حجة الوداع بحضرة جم غفير أزيد من مائة ألف ثم كتموا بعد ذلك وبايعوا أمير المؤمنين أبا بكر الصديق الأكبر، فانظر إلى سفاهتهم وحماقتهم، كيف ساغ لهم أن يقولوا مثل هذه المزخرفات فإنه لما جاز كتمان هذه الجماعة فقد أجازوا تواطؤهم على الكذب فيما هو أهم، بل عند هؤلاء الحمقى كتمان ما هو جزء ،الإيمان، وهذا يؤدي إلى أمور فظيعة شنيعة، فإنه إذن قد جاز وقوع معارضة القرآن لكنهم كتموا وقيام المعجزات على يد مسيلمة الكذاب، لكنهم كتموا ثم من أين وصل إليهم هذا الخبر إن نسبوه إلى أمير المؤمنين علي كرم الله وجهه، فهو خبر واحد غير مقبول عندهم مع أن الكذب يجوز عندهم تقية فيجوز أن يكون هذا من القبيل، كما أنهم قالوا إن إنكار علي تسليم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم الخلافة زماناً كان تقية وكذباً لغرض وإن تشبثوا بالعصمة، فمن أين يثبتون العصمة، لأن القرآن والأحاديث كلها صارت غير متواترة على أصلهم الكاسد ، فلم يبق في أيديهم إلا الدعاوى، ولا حماقة أشدّ من هذا، وهم كالسوفسطائية بل أشد منهم في إنكار الضروريات، وأشد من الملاحدة في إرادة هدم الشريعة الغراء، لكن الله متم نوره ولو كره الكافرون (لنا العادة قاضية به أي بالقطع بالكذب في مثل هذه الصورة فإن سكوت هذا الجم الغفير العظيم عن خبر علموه وكتمانهم ذلك مما تحيله العادة قطعاً كما لو انفرد بالخبر عن قتل الخطيب على المنبر بمشهد من أهل المدينة وسكوت أهل المدينة عن الإخبار به قطع بكذب المخبر المنفرد، لا سيما إذا لم يخبر أحد من المشاهدين، بل أخبروا خلافه ثم حدث الخبر بعدهم، كالخبر الذي
Issue: When one person alone narrates something for which there exist strong motives for transmission if it were true (and in the cause of knowledge many others shared with him) if they were witnesses, (his report is judged certainly false). The gist of the issue: one person's narration of a report that, if true, would have been known to many people, and none of those people narrated it at all, or one narrated and none among the others did, his report is certainly false, especially when the narrator claims that all or most shared knowledge of it. If you say: This would entail that a Companion is lying, God forbid, because the falsity of the report entails the falsity of the reporter. I say: This terrible entailment would only occur if such a narration actually occurred from the Companions, which is denied; you must investigate inductively. The author responded that it is attributed to forgetfulness, negligence, or error; in short, to a valid excuse. If not, then the narrator's falsity must be accepted, and the ruling on the Companion's justice is based on presumption and considered, as long as there is no evidence to the contrary. The correct view is to drop his last statement and suffice with attributing to forgetfulness and doubt, for the justice of the majority is certain. How can it be otherwise? Allah the Exalted has testified to the justice of the Companions of the Pledge of Ridwan, may Allah be pleased with them, and the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him and his family and companions, has testified to the justice of countless others, as is well known. This is contrary to the ugly Shi'a who claim the clear text on the Imamate of the Commander of the Faithful Ali after the Messenger of Allah; they say that the Messenger gave him the caliphate at Ghadir Khumm upon returning from the Farewell Pilgrimage in the presence of a great multitude exceeding one hundred thousand, then they concealed that afterwards and pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr. Look at their foolishness and idiocy: how could they say such embellished nonsense? If the concealment by this multitude is possible, then they have allowed their collusion in lying about what is most important. According to these fools, concealing what is part of faith leads to terrible results: then it would be possible that the Quran was opposed but they concealed it; that miracles occurred at the hand of Musaylima the liar but they concealed them. Then from where did this report reach them? If they attribute it to Ali, it is a solitary report, unacceptable according to them, especially since lying is allowed for them under dissimulation (taqiyya), so this could be from that category. Likewise, they say that Ali's denial of the Prophet's handing over the caliphate for a time was dissimulation and lies for a purpose. If they cling to infallibility, from where do they prove it? Because the Quran and hadiths all become non-mutawatir on their corrupt principle, so nothing remains in their hands but claims. There is no greater stupidity than this; they are like sophists, even worse in denying necessary truths, and worse than heretics in seeking to demolish the noble Shariah. But Allah will perfect His light, though the disbelievers dislike it. For us, custom decrees certainty of falsehood in such a situation, because the silence of this great multitude about a report they knew and their concealment of it is customarily impossible, just as if one alone reported the murder of a preacher on the pulpit in the presence of the people of Madinah and the people of Madinah remained silent about reporting it, then the report would be certainly false, especially when none of the witnesses reported it, rather they reported the opposite, then the report came later after them, like the report that...
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This extensive passage addresses the principle of 'solitary report when many would have known' (khabar al-wahid fi mā tatawaffaru al-dawā'ī). The Hanafi position is that if a single person reports an event that should have been witnessed by many, and no one else reports it, the report is certainly false (yuqta'u bi-kidhbihi). This is because custom ('adah) dictates that such important events cannot be concealed. The author applies this to refute the Shi'i claim of the Ghadir Khumm incident, arguing that if the Prophet had appointed Ali as successor in front of over 100,000 people, then it would have been widely reported; the fact that only some narrate it shows it is false. The text also addresses the concern about accusing Companions of lying, stating that this principle does not apply to them because such a situation never occurred; if a Companion reports something like this, it is attributed to forgetfulness. The justice of the majority of Companions is certain, as testified by Allah and the Prophet. Hence the Shi'i claim is rejected as contrary to established custom and logic, and their own reliance on isolated reports is inconsistent with their demand for certainty.

Key Terms
توفر الدواعي (tawaffur al-dawāʿī): There exist strong motives that would normally compel many to narrate an event if it occurred.
القطعي بالكذب (al-qaṭʿī bi al-kidhb): Certainty of falsity based on customary impossibility of concealment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul principle discussed, what is the ruling on a solitary report of an event that would have been witnessed by many and for which there are strong motives to transmit?
A) The report is accepted conditionally if the narrator is trustworthy
B) The report is certainly false (yuqta'u bi-kidhbihi)
C) The report is given the benefit of the doubt
D) The report is rejected only if the crowd denies it

Key Takeaway
When tawaffur al-dawa'i is present, a solitary report is certainly false; custom provides a rational proof for this ruling.

Al-Sunnah as Source
intermediate
12 min
The Principle of Tawaffur al-Dawa'i: Rejecting Single Reports of Public Events
Expounding the usul principle that a single narrator's claim of a public event is false when others remain silent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of tawaffur al-dawa'i to identify when a khabar wahid is definitively rejected and explain the rational proof based on customary impossibility.

Core Idea
If a public event is reported by only one person, the silence of all others proves the report is a lie.

This lesson unpacks a foundational Hanafi usul principle used to evaluate historical and legal reports. The principle states that when a matter is of such public nature that everyone would naturally transmit it, the fact that only one person reports it — while everyone else remains silent — makes the report a certain lie. This rational proof (dalil 'aqli) is applied to refute claims that contradict established tawatur, such as the Shi'a claim of the event of Ghadir Khumm. The passage also addresses objections and provides examples of miracles that were transmitted as ahad but are not rejected because they did not meet the condition of tawaffur al-dawa'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the principle
Note the claim: a single narrator reports something that would have been witnessed by many. The principle: if there are strong motives for transmission (tawaffur al-dawa'i), the silence of others proves the report false.
This is a rational proof, not scriptural.
2
Examine the rational proof
Understand why custom ('ada) rejects the possibility of widespread concealment, using the example of a murder in a public gathering (the preacher killed on the pulpit in Medina).
The impossibility is based on the nature of human behavior.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objection that silence could be due to motives (e.g., fear) is answered by showing that a universal, enduring motive covering everyone in all times is customarily impossible.
The text argues that fear of a few men cannot plausibly affect over 100,000 people for a lifetime.
4
Apply the principle to the Ghadir Khumm report
The Shi'a claim that the Prophet (ﷺ) publicly designated Ali as his successor at Ghadir Khumm is rejected because if it had occurred, the enormous crowd would have transmitted it; their silence proves it false.
This is a concrete application of the principle to a historical dispute.
5
Distinguish cases where ahad reports are accepted
Miracles like the splitting of the moon or the crying of the palm stump were witnessed by specific groups, not the entire community, so there were no strong motives for universal transmission; hence they can be accepted as ahad.
The principle only applies when the matter is of public interest and would naturally be transmitted by all.

The second principle: The Sunnah. Issue: When a single person reports something that there are strong motives to transmit (i.e., that would normally be known by many if it existed), and the cause of knowledge would have shared it with many people (if it existed, because they would be witnesses), then his report is definitively deemed a lie. The essence of the issue: a single individual narrates a report that, had it existed, many people would have known it, yet none of those many people narrated it at all, or one narrated it and no one else narrated it — such a report is definitively deemed a lie, especially if the reporter claims that all or most people share knowledge of it. If you say: this would imply that the Companion is a liar (God forbid), because the lie of the report entails the lie of the reporter. I say: the occurrence of this terrible consequence would only happen if Companions actually reported in this manner, which is denied. You must investigate. The author answered that it is attributed to forgetfulness, oversight, and error — in short, to a valid excuse. If not, then the reporter's lie must be accepted, and the ruling of the Companion's probity is presumptive and considered as long as no evidence of its absence exists. The truth is to drop his last statement and suffice with attributing it to forgetfulness and ambiguity, because the probity of the majority is definitive. How can it be otherwise, when God Almighty has testified to the probity of the Companions of the Pledge of Ridwan (may God be pleased with them) and the Messenger of God (peace be upon him and his family and companions) testified to the probity of countless people, as is not hidden? Contrary to the despicable Shi'a, who claim explicit textual designation (nass jali) for the Imamate of the Commander of the Faithful (Ali) after the Messenger of God (peace be upon him and his family and companions). They said: The Messenger of God gave him the caliphate at Ghadir Khumm when returning from the Farewell Pilgrimage in the presence of a huge crowd of over one hundred thousand, then they concealed it afterward and pledged allegiance to the Commander of the Faithful Abu Bakr al-Siddiq al-Akbar. Look at their foolishness and stupidity: how could they say such embellished things? Since they consider this multitude's concealment permissible, they have permitted their collusion in lying about what is most important. Rather, according to these fools, concealing what is part of faith leads to terrible and hideous consequences. For then it would be possible that the Quran was contradicted but they concealed it, and that miracles happened at the hand of Musaylimah the Liar but they concealed them. Then from where did this report reach them? If they attribute it to the Commander of the Faithful Ali (may God honor his face), it is a single report (khabar wahid) unacceptable to them, especially since lying is permissible for them as dissimulation (taqiyya), so this could be of that kind. Likewise, they said that Ali's denial of the Messenger's handing over the caliphate for a time was dissimulation and a lie for a purpose. Even if they cling to infallibility ('isma), how do they prove it? Because the Quran and all hadiths become non-mutawatir according to their corrupt principle, so nothing remains in their hands except claims. There is no stupidity greater than this. They are like the Sophists, even worse in denying necessities, and worse than the heretics in their desire to demolish the bright Sharia. But God will complete His light, though the disbelievers dislike it. (We say:) Custom ('ada) dictates definitive judgment of lying in such a case. Because the silence of this huge multitude about a matter they knew, and their concealment of it, is customarily impossible. For example, if one person reports the killing of the preacher on the pulpit while the people of Medina are witnessing it, and the people of Medina remain silent about reporting it, the lone reporter is definitively deemed a liar, especially if none of the witnesses reported it; rather they reported the opposite, and then the report occurs after them — like the report that the Rafidites (may God forsake them) claimed regarding the Imamate of the Commander of the Faithful, for it is absolutely a lie, as is necessary from the Rafidites. (They said:) Perhaps their silence was due to a motive that compelled them to conceal it; but silence does not yield certainty unless the absence of motives is known, and motives for concealment are many and cannot be enumerated. So silence does not prove lying. Do you not see that the Christians did not transmit the speech of Jesus (peace be upon our Prophet and him) in the cradle, when the people came to Mary accusing her, and he admonished them: 'He said: I am the servant of God. He has given me the Scripture and made me a prophet. He has made me blessed wherever I am, and has enjoined upon me prayer and zakah as long as I live, and dutiful to my mother, and He has not made me a tyrant wretched. And peace be upon me the day I was born, the day I die, and the day I am raised alive.' [Maryam 30-33] And the splitting of the moon was transmitted as ahad from Ibn Mas'ud who said: 'The moon split on the day of the Messenger of God into two parts, one part above the mountain and one part below, and the Messenger of God said: Bear witness.' It was narrated by al-Bukhari and Muslim. And the glorification of pebbles and food: from Ibn Mas'ud (may God be pleased with him) who said: We used to count the signs as blessings, while you count them as a warning. And he said: We were with the Messenger of God on a journey, and water became scarce. He said: 'Look for some extra water.' They brought a little water in a vessel. He put his hand in the vessel and said: 'Come to the pure and blessed water, and blessing is from God.' I saw water gushing from between his fingers. And we used to hear the glorification of food while it was being eaten. Narrated by al-Bukhari. And the yearning of the stump: from Jabir (may God be pleased with him) who said: The Messenger of God used to lean against a date-palm stump from the pillars of the mosque when giving the sermon. When the pulpit was made and he stood on it, the palm tree that he used to lean against cried out until it nearly split. The Prophet came down and embraced it, and it began to moan like a child being silenced until it settled. He said: It wept for what it used to hear of remembrance. Narrated by al-Bukhari. And the running of the tree: from Ibn 'Umar (may God be pleased with them both) who said: We were with the Messenger of God on a journey. A Bedouin came. When he approached, the Messenger of God said to him: 'Where are you going?' He said: To my family. He said: 'Will you not bear witness to what I say?' He said: Who will bear witness to what you say? He said: 'This acacia tree.' He called it while he was on the bank of the valley, and it came, ploughing the ground, until it stood before him. He asked it to bear witness three times, and it bore witness three times that he is as he said. Then it returned to its root. Narrated by al-Darimi. And the greeting of the stone and the gazelle: from Jabir ibn Samurah that the Messenger of God said: 'There is a stone in Mecca that used to greet me during the nights I was sent; and I recognize it.' And many different branches, such as the adhan being two by two, etc. (Ahad) is related to 'narrated': these matters were narrated as ahad despite the silence of the rest, and they are not definitively deemed lies. (The answer:) The existence of a motive covering all, far and near, in every time and place, as they said, is customarily non-existent. The issue is when they remained silent for a lifetime. Furthermore, the motive they mentioned for concealing the report of the Imamate is fear of the three caliphs and the remaining ten. Look at their foolishness: how could a crowd of over one hundred thousand fear a countable number of men, and how could this fear persist even after their deaths (may God be pleased with them)? Then
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a key usul principle: when a single narrator reports an event that would normally have been witnessed and transmitted by many (i.e., there are strong motives for widespread transmission), the very fact that no one else reports it — or even that others contradict it — definitively proves the report false. The commentator addresses the objection that this would impugn the honesty of a Companion narrator. He resolves it by explaining that if such a situation occurred from a Companion, it would be attributed to forgetfulness or error, not deliberate falsehood. He then launches into a refutation of the Shi'a claim about the event of Ghadir Khumm, using exactly this principle: if such a public event had occurred, the thousands present would have transmitted it, and their silence proves the claim is a lie. The commentator also rebuts the objection that silence could be due to motives like fear, arguing that a universal, enduring motive covering all people in all times is customarily impossible. He provides examples of miraculous events (splitting of the moon, etc.) that were transmitted as ahad from a few Companions, but the silence of others does not disprove them because there were no strong motives for every witness to transmit (the events were witnessed by specific groups, not the entire community). The core ruling is that this principle applies only when the matter is of such public nature that transmission would be expected. It is a rational proof based on customary impossibility.

Key Terms
توفر الدواعي (tawaffur al-dawa'i): The existence of strong motives for widespread transmission; when a report is of such importance that people would naturally transmit it.
سكوت الباقين (sukut al-baqin): The silence of the remaining people who should have transmitted the report; this silence, when no plausible motive for concealment exists, proves the report false.
خبر واحد (khabar wahid): A report transmitted by one person or a few, not reaching tawatur; in this context, such a report is rejected if it contradicts what would be expected from tawatur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul principle discussed in this passage, if a single narrator reports that a public event occurred, and all other potential witnesses remain silent, what is the ruling?
A) The report is accepted because the narrator is presumably trustworthy.
B) The report is definitively rejected as a lie because the silence of the masses, under normal circumstances, proves it false.
C) The report is considered mutawatir because the single narrator's report is sufficient.
D) The report is accepted only if the narrator is a Companion.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of tawaffur al-dawa'i provides a rational proof to reject reports of public events when the masses remain silent, preserving the reliability of tawatur.
