أسن مني بستة أشهر، فجميع مسموعاته أيضاً قبل البلوغ وأما أنس بن مالك فكان ابن عشر سنين حين قدم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم وابن عشرين حين توفي، فكان أكثر مسموعاته قبل البلوغ، وقد قبلوه كله وأما ما روى ابن عبد البر أنه شهد بدراً فما كان شهوده للقتال بل لخدمة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم، فإنه كان خادماً له الله عنه، فقد بان لك أن الأوجه في الاستدلال أن يقال : لأنهم مسموعاتهم رضوان الله عليهم قبل البلوغ فافهم. وقد استدل بأنه جرت عادة السلف بإسماع الصبيان، فلو لم تقبل مسموعاتهم لما كان له فائدة ولم يرتض به المص ( و ) قال (أما الإسماع للصبيان فغير مستلزم) للمطلوب لاحتمال التبرك والاعتياد بالرواية وهما من أعظم الفوائد (وقيل: المراهق مقبول)
رضي
،
Introduction: On the conditions of narration. Among them is intellect and discernment for receiving, i.e., for receiving hadith. And they differed in determining the minimum years by which discernment is acquired. It was said: five – and this is the chosen view of Ibn al-Salah, as Mahmud ibn al-Rabi' said: I understood a spit that the Messenger of God (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions) spat, and I was five years old – narrated by al-Bukhari. And it was said: four, due to the hadith of the spit, and this claimant alleged that the correct view is that Mahmud ibn al-Rabi' was four in the sahih. And it does not follow that at the age of four or five one is discerning, nor that all children at this age are discerning. And it was said: the minimum is fifteen, and this is transmitted from Ibn Ma'in. Imam Ahmad said: this is amazing from him. And the truth is that the mentioned estimates do not deserve attention, and the most correct is not to estimate by age, for the intellect strengthens little by little from divine mercy, and man cannot estimate a specific amount of it, and this amount varies among children. Rather, the estimate is by understanding speech and responding. So when the child is such that he understands speech and responds, he is fit to receive hadith. But generally, he is not in this state before reaching seven, so guardians are ordered to command them to pray when they reach this age. As for Mahmud al-Rabi' understanding at five or four, and Imam al-Shafi'i memorizing al-Muwatta' when he was five, and Imam Sahl ibn 'Abd Allah al-Tustari memorizing some litanies when he was two, these are among the miracles; the general rule is not built upon them. Yes, if one is found with these attributes, his reception is absolutely accepted, and therefore we did not stipulate a specific age, but it is rarely found. So understand. And the condition for performance is perfection of intellect, and this also varies among men, so a specific amount cannot be assigned. So its cause has been made to stand in its place legally, as with travel and hardship. He said: and its standard is puberty, free from insanity and madness. And we only stipulated discernment itself for reception and its perfection for performance, by analogy with testimony, because both are reports. And because they accepted the narrations of 'Abd Allah ibn 'Abbas, 'Abd Allah ibn al-Zubayr, al-Nu'man ibn Bashir, and Anas ibn Malik without asking them whether they received before puberty or after. So it is known that puberty is not a condition for reception, while its stipulation for performance will have its justification later. And know that 'Abd Allah ibn 'Abbas was born three years before the Hijrah according to al-Waqidi, so his age at the Prophet's death was thirteen years. Also in al-Isti'ab. And Ibn 'Abd al-Barr said about him: We have narrated from multiple chains from Sa'id ibn Jubayr from Ibn 'Abbas: The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) died and I was ten years old, and I had read al-Muhkam, meaning al-Mufassal. Then it was narrated by another chain from the path of 'Abd Allah from his father with his chain from Ibn 'Abbas: The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) died and I was fifteen years old. And al-Bukhari narrated that he was approaching puberty during the days of the Farewell Pilgrimage. So then, his puberty at the time of reception is not confirmed at all. Likewise 'Abd Allah ibn al-Zubayr, the first child born in Islam, after the Hijrah by one or two years according to the well-known view, and it was said in the first year. So all his listening was before puberty, for the Prophet's stay in Medina was ten years. Likewise al-Nu'man ibn Bashir, the first child born among the Ansar after the Hijrah, and the Prophet died while he was eight years old, and it was said six. Ibn 'Abd al-Barr said in al-Isti'ab: the first is more correct, God willing, because most say that he and Ibn al-Zubayr were born two years after the Hijrah. And al-Tabari narrated that Ibn al-Zubayr said: al-Nu'man ibn Bashir and 'Abd Allah... older than me by six months. So all his listening was also before puberty. As for Anas ibn Malik, he was ten years old when the Prophet came to Medina and twenty when he died, so most of his listening was before puberty, and they accepted all of it. As for what Ibn 'Abd al-Barr narrated that he witnessed Badr, his witnessing was not for fighting but for serving the Prophet (peace be upon him), for he was his servant, may God be pleased with him. So it has become clear to you that the best way of inference is to say: because their narrations (peace be upon them) were before puberty – so understand. And it was argued that the practice of the Salaf was to make children listen, so if their narrations were not accepted, there would be no benefit in that. And the author did not approve. And he said: As for making children listen, it is not necessitating of the desired conclusion, due to the possibility of seeking blessing and accustoming to narration, and these are among the greatest benefits. And it was said: the adolescent is accepted.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage from the sharh introduces the conditions for narrating hadith (شرائط الرواية). The first condition is discrimination (tamyiz) for reception (tahammul). The author summarizes the scholarly disagreement on the minimum age for tamyiz: some say 5 years (Ibn al-Salah, based on the hadith of Mahmud ibn al-Rabi'), others 4 years, others 15 years (attributed to Ibn Ma'in). The correct view, however, is that no specific age is fixed; rather, the criterion is the child's actual ability to understand speech and respond appropriately. This generally occurs around age 7, which is why children are commanded to pray at that age. Exceptional cases of early understanding (e.g., Mahmud at 4/5, al-Shafi'i at 5, al-Tustari at 2) are miracles and not the basis for the general rule. For performance (ada'), complete intellect is required, and its legal standard is puberty (bulugh) free from insanity, analogous to testimony. The passage then provides evidence from the Sahaba: Ibn 'Abbas, Ibn al-Zubayr, al-Nu'man ibn Bashir, and Anas ibn Malik all narrated hadith that they had received before puberty, and the Muslim community accepted their narrations. This proves that puberty is not a condition for tahammul. Detailed biographical notes on Ibn 'Abbas and others are given to show that their pre-pubescent listening is well-attested. The final part discusses an objection: if the Salaf made children listen, it might be for baraka or practice, not necessarily for acceptance of their narrations. The author responds that although that is possible, the fact that they accepted their narrations without inquiry shows that the intention was acceptance. The text concludes with a note that some consider the adolescent (murahiq) acceptable as well.

Key Terms
التمييز (al-tamyiz): The ability to understand speech and respond appropriately, required for tahammul of hadith
التحمل (al-tahammul): The act of receiving and memorizing hadith
الأداء (al-ada'): The act of transmitting hadith

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about the narrations of the Companions who heard hadith before puberty?
A) They were accepted only if they were close to puberty.
B) They were accepted without inquiry, proving that puberty is not a condition for reception.
C) They were accepted only if the narrator was an adult at the time of narration.
D) They were rejected because of their young age.

Key Takeaway
The Sahaba's practice establishes that puberty is required only for performance, not for reception.

Rejecting Narration from a Minor: The Hanafi Position and Refutation of the Quba Incident
Ruling on accepting narration from a minor and rebuttal of the Quba incident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a minor's narration is rejected unless subjected to scrutiny, and refute the Quba counterargument by identifying the actual adult informant.

Core Idea
A minor's narration is rejected due to lack of legal responsibility; the Quba incident does not prove otherwise.

In this passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut, the author establishes the Hanafi position that the narration of a minor (murahiq) is not accepted due to the suspicion of lying, since a minor is not legally accountable. He then addresses a key counterargument: the incident where the people of Quba allegedly accepted a narration from Anas ibn Malik and Ibn Umar when they were minors. The refutation proceeds by examining the ages of the narrators and demonstrating that the actual informant was an adult.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi rule and its basis
The passage states that the narration of a minor is not accepted absolutely, only with scrutiny (taharrī). The reason is the suspicion of lack of taklif (legal responsibility), similar to the rejection of a sinner's narration but even more so.
2
Understand the objection based on the Quba incident
Some scholars argue that the people of Quba accepted a report about the change of qibla from Anas and Ibn Umar when they were minors, proving that a minor's narration can be accepted.
3
Analyze the Hanafi refutation of the ages
The author shows that Anas was ten at the Prophet's arrival and the qibla change came later, so he was still a minor. Ibn Umar was not yet an adult at Badr. Thus both were minors.
4
Identify the actual adult informant
The passage argues that the real informant was 'Ubbad ibn Bishr or 'Ubbad ibn Nuhayk, who were adults. Anas and Ibn Umar were only narrators of the story, not the informants to the people of Quba.
5
Conclude why the Quba incident is not a valid proof
Even if the informants were Anas or Ibn Umar, the acceptance by the people of Quba was based on their personal faith (iman bi al-ghayb), not a legal precedent. Thus it does not establish a rule.

رواية وأداء، لكن لا مطلقاً بل ( مع التحري) فإن وقع على الصدق يقبل وإلا لا (و) قال في «التحرير» لإبطاله المعتمد في هذا الباب الصحابة، ولم يرجعوا إليه) أي المراهق فهو غير مقبول (أقول غاية ما لزم منه فقدان الدليل، ولا يلزم من انتفاء الدليل انتفاء المدلول) وهو غير واضح، فإنه يستدل بانتفاء المراجعة على عدم قبوله فعدم الرجوع دليل عليه فالأحرى يقال : لعل عدم المراجعة لعدم حاجتهم إلى المراجعة إليهم فلا يصلح حجة (بل الوجه) في (إبطاله) تهمة عدم التكليف فإن المراهق غير مكلف، فلا الكذب عليه، فيجوز أن يحرم أن وهو إذ ذاك يكذب، فلهذه التهمة لا يقبل كما في الفاسق بل أولى قال من يقبل المراهق أن أهل قباء قبلوا أنس مالك وابن عمر وهما إذ ذاك غير بالغين فأجاب بقوله : (واعتماد أهل قباء على بن أنس أو ابن عمر بسن البلوغ على الأصح) وقد عرفت أن أنساً كان يوم القدوم الشريف ابن عشر، وكان تحول القبلة بعده بستة عشر شهراً أو سبعة عشر كما في «صحيح البخاري»، فكيف يكون بالغاً؟ وأما ابن عمر ففي الاستيعاب : قال الواقدي : كان يوم بدر ممن لم يحتلم، فاستصغره رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم ،ورده، فكيف يكون بالغاً عند تحول القبلة، وأعجب من هذا ما قيل إن الصحيح أن المخبر أنس وابن عمر معاً، بالغ وأسن من ابن عمر وقبلوا قوله فإنه قد بان لك أن الأمر بالعكس، فالحق في الجواب أن أنساً وابن عمر راويان والمخبر غيرهما، فإنه في رواية أنس : فمر رجل من بني سلمة وهم ركوع في صلاة الفجر، وفي رواية ابن عمر بينما الناس بقباء بصلاة الصبح إذ جاءهم آت إلى الآخر، فقيل: هو عباد بن بشر في التيسير»، قال الحافظ العسقلاني : إنه أرجح رواه ابن خيثمة، وفي «التحرير»: هو عباد بن نهيك عند المحدثين، وهو شيخ كبير، وعباد بن بشر قتل شهيداً اليمامة وهو ابن خمس وأربعين سنة ونهيك ككريم والذي يظهر لهذا العبد أنه يوم المخبر لأهل مسجد بني حارثة لا لأهل مسجد قباء والله أعلم . ثم لو سلم أن المخبر إياهم أنس أو ابن عمر فغاية ما لزم قبول أهل قباء، هو ليس حجة، فإن تشبث بتقرير النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فممنوع، بل قال هم آمنوا بالغيب فلم يعد هذا الخبر خبراً فافهم.
Narration and performance, but not absolutely rather with scrutiny. If it occurs truthful it is accepted, otherwise not. And he said in Al-Tahrir: To invalidate it, the relied-upon in this chapter are the Companions, and they did not refer to him, meaning the minor, so he is not accepted. I say: The utmost that follows from it is lack of evidence, and from the absence of evidence does not necessarily follow the absence of the indicated. And it is unclear, for they infer from the absence of returning to them that it is not accepted, so the non-return is evidence against it. Rather it is more appropriate to say: Perhaps the lack of return is due to their not needing to return to them, so it is not a valid proof. Rather the proper aspect in invalidating it is the suspicion of lack of legal responsibility, for the minor is not legally responsible, so lying is not upon him, so it is possible that he lies at that time. So due to this suspicion he is not accepted, like the sinner, even more so. He who accepted the minor said that the people of Quba accepted Anas ibn Malik and Ibn Umar while they were then not yet adults. He replied saying: The reliance of the people of Quba on Anas ibn Malik or Ibn Umar at the age of puberty as the correct view. And you know that Anas was ten years old on the day of the noble arrival, and the change of the qibla was after sixteen or seventeen months as in Sahih al-Bukhari, so how could he be an adult? As for Ibn Umar, in Al-Isti‘ab: Al-Waqidi said: He was on the day of Badr among those who had not had a wet dream, so the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, considered him young and rejected him, so how could he be an adult at the time of the change of the qibla? And more surprising than this is what was said: The correct view is that the informant is Anas and Ibn Umar together, an adult and older than Ibn Umar, and they accepted his report. But it has become clear to you that the matter is the opposite. The truth in the answer is that Anas and Ibn Umar are narrators, and the informant is someone else. For in Anas's narration: A man from Banu Salama passed by while they were bowing in the Fajr prayer. And in Ibn Umar's narration: While the people in Quba were praying Fajr, someone came to them... And it was said: He is 'Ubbad ibn Bishr in Al-Taysir. Al-Hafiz al-'Asqalani said: It is most likely, narrated by Ibn Khaythama. And in Al-Tahrir: He is 'Ubbad ibn Nuhayk among the hadith scholars, and he was an old man. 'Ubbad ibn Bishr was martyred in Yamama at the age of forty-five. And Nuhayk is like Kuraym. And what appears to this servant is that the informant was for the people of the mosque of Bani Haritha, not for the people of the mosque of Quba. And Allah knows best. Then if it is conceded that the informant to them was Anas or Ibn Umar, the utmost that follows is the acceptance of the people of Quba, which is not a proof. For if he clings to the tacit approval of the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, it is rejected. Rather they believed in the unseen, so this report did not become a report. Understand.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the acceptance of narration from a minor (murahiq). The author argues that a minor's narration is not accepted unless it is subjected to scrutiny (taharrī) and found to be truthful. The relied-upon position is that Companions did not refer to minors. The objection that the people of Quba accepted narrations from Anas and Ibn Umar when they were minors is refuted: first, Anas was ten at the time of the Prophet's arrival but the qibla change was 16-17 months later, and Ibn Umar was below puberty at Badr, so both were minors. Second, the actual informant was a different adult (e.g., 'Ubbad ibn Bishr or 'Ubbad ibn Nuhayk). Even if they were the informants, the acceptance by the people of Quba is not a binding proof; they acted on faith, not established legal practice. Thus, the Hanafi position rejecting a minor's narration stands due to suspicion of lying since the minor is not legally responsible.

Key Terms
مراهق (murahiq): A minor nearing the age of puberty, whose narration is rejected due to lack of taklif (legal responsibility).
تحري (taharrī): Scrutiny to determine the truthfulness of a narration, applied only if the minor's narration is considered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the narration of a minor (murahiq) rejected according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the minor cannot memorize properly.
B) Because the minor is not held accountable for lying, creating suspicion.
C) Because the Companions never narrated from minors.
D) Because minors are not allowed to speak about religious matters.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects a minor's narration due to lack of taklif, and the Quba incident does not establish a counter-precedent because the informant was an adult.

Testimony and Narration of People of Innovation
States the Hanafi ruling on shahada and riwaya of ahl al-ahwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on accepting shahada and riwaya from ahl al-ahwa and distinguish between the two categories.

Core Idea
Hanafis accept testimony of innovators but reject their narrations if they call to innovation.

This passage from a Hanafi usul work presents the school's nuanced position on the reliability of people of innovation (ahl al-ahwa). The key is understanding that testimony (shahada) and transmission (riwaya) are treated differently. While the school generally accepts testimony from innovators (except the extremist Khattabiyya), it rejects narrations from those who actively propagate their innovation. This distinction rests on the rationale that calling to innovation leads to fabrication, whereas testimony in legal rights does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and act
The passage deals with ahl al-ahwa (people of desire/innovation) and two legal acts: testifying (shahada) and narrating (riwaya). Understand each independently.
The passage treats shahada and riwaya separately.
2
State the Hanafi rule for shahada
Testimony is accepted from all ahl al-ahwa except the Khattabiyya and those who consider ilham as proof. The reason: deep religiosity prevents lying.
Exception: Khattabiyya because they permit false testimony for their sect.
3
State the Hanafi rule for riwaya
Narration is not accepted from one who adopts an innovation and calls people to it. This is agreed upon by all imams of fiqh and hadith.
Calling to innovation leads to fabrication (taqawwul), so trust is lost.
4
Understand the rationale behind the distinction
Shahada concerns rights of people (huquq al-nas), where false testimony is less likely due to lack of motive. Riwaya concerns religious knowledge, where calling to innovation creates motive to fabricate.
The rationale is key: motive and domain differ.
5
Notice the opposing view and the chosen position
Al-Amidi (Shafi'i), Imam Malik, and most other Hanafis reject testimony of innovators altogether. The text declares the Hanafi position (acceptance except Khattabiyya) as 'al-mukhtar' (the chosen).
The author sides with the majority of later Hanafis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في البدعة الجلية القبول عند الأكثر غير محققي الحنفية (وهو المختار) عند من تلاهم (خلافاً للآمدي) من الشافعية (ومن يتبعه) والإمام مالك ومعظم الحنفية، وهو المختار عند هذا العبد. قال الإمام فخر الإسلام وأما صاحب الهوى فإن أصحابنا عملوا بشهاداتهم إلا الخطابية، لأن صاحب الهوى، وإنما وقع فيه لتعمقه، وذلك يصده عن الكذب، فلم يصلح شبهة إلا من تدين بتصديق المدعي إذا كان ينتحل بنحلته فيتهم بالباطل والزور مثل الخطابية، وكذلك من قال : الإلهام حجة يجب أن لا تقبل شهادته أيضاً، وأما في السنن فقيل إن المذهب المختار عندنا أن لا تقبل رواية من انتحل الهوى والبدعة ودعا الناس إليه على هذا أئمة الفقه والحديث كلهم، لأن المحاجة والدعوة إلى الهوى سبب داع على التقول فلا يؤتمن على حديثه، وليس كذلك الشهادة في حقوق الناس، لأن ذلك لا يدعو إلى التزوير في ذلك الباب، فلم ترد شهادته، فإذا هذا كان صاحب الهوى بمنزلة الفاسق في باب السنن والأحايث انتهى كلماته الشريفة.
In clear innovation, acceptance [of testimony] is [the position] of the majority of non-verifying Hanafis, and it is the chosen [view] of those after them, contrary to al-Amidi from the Shafi'is and those who follow him, and Imam Malik and most Hanafis, and it is the chosen [view] of this servant. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said: As for the person of desire, our companions acted upon their testimonies except al-Khattabiyya, because the person of desire fell into it only due to his deep involvement, and that prevents him from lying, so no suspicion arises except from one who makes it his religion to believe the claimant if he adopts his sect, so he is accused of falsehood and perjury like al-Khattabiyya. Similarly, whoever says that inspiration is a proof, his testimony must not be accepted. As for [the transmission of] the Sunan, it is said that the chosen school with us is not to accept the narration of one who adopts desire and innovation and calls people to it. Upon this are the imams of fiqh and hadith all of them, because argumentation and calling to desire is a cause that leads to fabrication, so he is not trustworthy in his hadith. It is not like testimony in people's rights, because that does not lead to forgery in that chapter, so his testimony is not rejected. Thus, the person of desire is like a transgressor in the chapter of the Sunan and hadiths. End of his noble words.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (hukm) from a Hanafi usul work on the acceptance of testimony (shahada) and narration (riwaya) from people of innovation (ahl al-bid'ah). It begins by stating that in the case of clear innovation (bid'ah jalilah), the majority of Hanafis who are not the verifying scholars accept their testimony, and this is the chosen position of later scholars, contrary to al-Amidi (a Shafi'i) and those who follow him, as well as Imam Malik and most Hanafis (who reportedly reject it). The author then says this is his own chosen view. He quotes Imam Fakhr al-Islam, who explains that the Hanafis (our companions) have acted upon the testimonies of people of desire (ahl al-ahwa) except for the Khattabiyya (an extremist Shia sect). The reason is that such a person fell into desire due to deep thinking, and this very religiosity prevents him from lying. However, the Khattabiyya are excluded because they consider it permissible to testify falsely for their co-religionists. Similarly, anyone who considers divine inspiration (ilham) as a proof (hujja) should not have his testimony accepted. For the Sunan (hadith narrations), the chosen school (madhhab mukhtar) within the Hanafi school is not to accept the narration of one who adopts desire and innovation and calls people to it. All imams of fiqh and hadith agree on this, because arguing and calling to desire leads to fabrication (taqawwul), so such a narrator is not trustworthy. The author notes a distinction: testimony in people's rights (huquq al-nas) does not lead to forgery, so it is accepted, but narrations about religious matters are more sensitive, so the innovator who calls to his innovation is treated like a fasiq (transgressor) in this context. This concludes the quote from Fakhr al-Islam, and the author seems to endorse this view.

Key Terms
صاحب الهوى (sahib al-hawa): A person who follows his desire and adopts an innovative belief (bid'ah), even if not necessarily calling to it.
البدعة الجلية (al-bid'ah al-jaliyyah): A clear and manifest innovation, as opposed to subtle innovation (ghayr jaliyyah).
الخطابية (al-Khattabiyya): A sect of ghulat (extremist) Shia who follow Abu al-Khattab, known for allowing false testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi ruling on accepting the narration (riwaya) of a person who holds an innovative belief and calls others to it?
A) His narration is accepted because innovation does not affect trustworthiness.
B) His narration is rejected because calling to innovation leads to fabrication.
C) His narration is accepted only if he testifies in court.
D) His narration is rejected only if he is from the Khattabiyya.

Key Takeaway
Hanafis accept testimony from innovators (except Khattabiyya) but reject narrations from those who actively propagate their innovation.

Critique of Distinguishing da'iyah from Non-da'iyah in Narrator Criticism
Critical refinement of the da'iyah vs. non-da'iyah distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on accepting narrations from innovators and evaluate the rational proof that all innovators are da'iyah.

Core Idea
Every innovator is necessarily a caller; thus all narrations from innovators are rejected unconditionally.

This passage presents a critical refinement of a key usul dispute: whether an innovator who does not actively call to his innovation can be considered reliable in hadith narration. The commentator argues forcefully that such a distinction is invalid because the very nature of religious innovation compels its adherent to be a caller. Understanding this argument illuminates the Hanafi school's rigorous approach to preserving the sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
First, state the position attributed to Fakhr al-Islam (all innovators' narrations rejected) and the opposing view of sahib al-Kashf (only those who actively call are rejected).
The commentator aligns with the first view, but adds a stronger rationale.
2
Analyze the rhetorical device: iqamat al-zahir mawdi' al-mudmar
The commentator explains that Fakhr al-Islam's phrase 'man intahala' (who adopts) is a manifest expression placed to imply that all people of desire are adopters and callers. Understand how this device makes the ruling general.
This is a linguistic argument: the manifest stands for the implicit to include all cases.
3
Examine the rational proof (dalil 'aqli)
The commentator provides a rational argument: any innovator believes his innovation is correct and considers commanding good (al-amr bi al-ma'ruf) obligatory. Therefore, he must necessarily invite others to his view; otherwise he contradicts his own religious duty.
This proof does not rely on textual evidence but on necessary implication from the innovator's belief.
4
Critique the opposing evidence
The opposing side cites examples of Bukhari and Muslim narrating from known innovators. The commentator refutes this by noting the issue is disputed and that great imams like Muhammad ibn Sirin refrained from such narrations, so the practice of some is not a proof.
The argument shifts from examples to authority: consensus or majority practice is preponderant.
5
Reject further interpretations
The commentator rejects the view that Fakhr al-Islam's statement only applied to subtle innovation (ghayr jaliyyah) while clear innovation (jaliyyah) would be accepted. He uses a min al-qiyas al-awla argument: if subtle innovation causes rejection, clear innovation is even more deserving.
This step shows the principle of qiyas al-awla (argument a fortiori) in usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندي : أن قوله من انتحل من قبيل إقامة الظاهر موضع المضمر والحاصل أن أصحاب البدع لا تقبل روايتهم كما يدل عليه قوله آخراً، وإنما أقام الظاهر تنبيهاً على أن أصحاب الهوى كلهم منتحلون البدعة داعون إليها، فلا تقبل روايتهم في أمر ديني أصلاً، كما روي عن محمد بن سيرين السنن دينكم، فانظروا عمن تأخذون دينكم، وصاحب الكشف» حمل كلامه على أن صاحب البدعة إن كان داعياً للناس إلى بدعته لا يقبل وإلا يقبل والذي حمله على هذا الحمل وجد في الصحاح روايات عن أصحاب البدعة، فإن محمد بن إسماعيل البخاري روى في صحيحه عن عباد بن يعقوب، وقال الإمام أبو بكر بن إسحاق بن خزيمة : حدثنا الصادق في روايته المتهم في دينه عباد بن يعقوب، واحتج البخاري بمحمد بن زياد وحريز بن عثمان، وقد اشتهر عنهما النصب، وقد اتفق البخاري ومسلم على الاحتجاج بأبي معاوية محمد بن حازم بن موسى وقد اشتهر عنهما الغلو وفيه نظر ظاهر، فإن صاحب الهوى لما زعم في زعمه الباطل أنه الصواب وأنه الشريعة المحمدية، وأن الأمر بالمعروف فرض عنده، فلا بد أن يكون للناس داعياً إلى هواه ففرض أنه ليس بداع إلى هواه إما محال وإما مناف للعدالة لإتيانه محذور دينه في زعمه وأيضاً ينافيه كلامه في آخر البحث، وإذا صح الهوى بمنزلة الفاسق وتخريج محمد بن إسماعيل البخاري ومسلم ومحمد بن إسحاق لا حجة فيه، فإن المسألة مختلف فيها، فلا يكون زعم أحد الفريقين حجة على الآخر، كيف ومثل الإمام إمام أئمة الحديث محمد بن سيرين كف الرواية عنهم، وأعجب من هذا الحمل ما حمل عليه البعض من أن ما قال : إنما هو في البدعة الغير الجلية وأما في البدعة الجلية فتقبل روايتهم وإن كانوا داعين كيف يصح ولا يساعده أول كلامه وليس له عين ولا أثر، ولما كانت الدعوة إلى البدعة الغير الجلية موجبة للتهمة وعدم القبول فإن الجلية بالطريق الأولى، فقد بان لك أن رواية أهل البدع مطلقاً لا تقبل عند هذا الحبر الإمام الهمام.
In my view: His statement 'who adopts' is of the type of placing the manifest in the position of the implicit. The conclusion is that the narrations of the people of innovation are not accepted, as his statement indicates at the end. He only placed the manifest as a warning that all people of desire are adopters of innovation and callers to it, so their narrations in any religious matter are not accepted at all, as narrated from Muhammad ibn Sirin: 'The Sunan are your religion, so look from whom you take your religion.' The author of al-Kashf interpreted his words to mean that if the innovator is a caller to his innovation, he is not accepted; otherwise, he is accepted. What led him to this interpretation is that he found in the Sahihs narrations from innovators: Muhammad ibn Isma'il al-Bukhari narrated in his Sahih from 'Abbad ibn Ya'qub, and Imam Abu Bakr ibn Ishaq ibn Khuzaymah said: 'The truthful in his narration, the accused in his religion, 'Abbad ibn Ya'qub narrated to us.' Bukhari also used as evidence Muhammad ibn Ziyad and Hariz ibn 'Uthman, both known for Nasb, and Bukhari and Muslim agreed on using as evidence Abu Mu'awiyah Muhammad ibn Hazim ibn Musa, known for Ghulu. There is apparent weakness in this. For the person of desire, since he claims in his false belief that his view is correct and that it is the Shari'ah of Muhammad, and that commanding good is obligatory in his view, he must necessarily call people to his desire. To assume he does not call to his desire is either impossible or contradictory to his 'adala (probity) because he commits what he considers a religious violation. Also, it contradicts his statement at the end of the research. If it is established that the person of desire is like a fasiq, then the citations from al-Bukhari, Muslim, and Muhammad ibn Ishaq are not a proof, because the issue is disputed, so the claim of one party is not a proof against the other. How can it be, when the likes of the imam of the imams of hadith, Muhammad ibn Sirin, refrained from narrating from them? More amazing than this interpretation is what some have interpreted that his statement refers only to subtle innovation (ghayr al-jaliyyah), but in clear innovation (jaliyyah), their narrations are accepted even if they are callers. How can that be correct? It is not supported by the first part of his speech, and it has no substance or trace. Since calling to subtle innovation is sufficient to cause suspicion and rejection, then clear innovation is even more so. Thus, it has become clear to you that the narrations of the people of innovation are unconditionally rejected according to this great imam.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hishiya) by the commentator. He addresses the earlier statement by Fakhr al-Islam regarding not accepting narrations from those who adopt desire and innovation and call people to it. The commentator argues that the phrase 'who adopts' (man intahala) is actually a metonymy (iqamat al-zahir maqam al-mudmar): the manifest phrase 'who adopts' is placed in the position of the implicit meaning that all people of desire are adopters and callers. Therefore, the ruling is that narrations from all people of innovation are rejected, not just from those who actively call. He supports this with the famous quote from Muhammad ibn Sirin: 'The Sunan are your religion, so look from whom you take your religion.' The commentator then critiques the view of 'sahib al-Kashf' (likely al-Kashf al-Asrar or similar) who distinguishes between an innovator who is a caller (da'iyah) and one who is not, accepting the latter's narration. This view attempts to justify the fact that Bukhari and Muslim narrated from some individuals who were known innovators (like 'Abbad ibn Ya'qub, who was a Shi'i, and others with Nasb or Ghulu). The commentator refutes this by reasoning that any innovator who believes his innovation is correct and that it is the true Shari'ah, and considers commanding good (al-amr bi al-ma'ruf) obligatory, must necessarily be a caller to his innovation. To assume otherwise is impossible or contradicts his own probity ('adala). Thus, the distinction between caller and non-caller is invalid, and all innovators are callers. Therefore, their narrations are unconditionally rejected. He also dismisses the examples from Bukhari and Muslim as not being proof because the issue is disputed; one cannot use the practice of some imams as a proof against the consensus of many others, like Muhammad ibn Sirin, who refrained. Finally, he rejects a further interpretation that Fakhr al-Islam's statement was only about subtle innovation (ghayr jaliyyah), whereas for clear innovation, their narrations are accepted even if they are callers. The commentator says this is even more untenable.

Key Terms
إقامة الظاهر موضع المضمر (iqamat al-zahir mawdi' al-mudmar): A rhetorical device where the manifest word is placed in the position of the implicit meaning to emphasize that the manifest stands for all cases.
الداعية (al-da'iyah): One who calls others to his innovation or belief.
صاحب الكشف (sahib al-kashf): The author of the work 'al-Kashf' (likely al-Kashf 'an al-Haqa'iq or similar), who holds the distinction between calling and non-calling innovators.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does he reject the distinction between a calling and a non-calling innovator?
A) Because the non-calling innovator is more trustworthy.
B) Because the innovator's belief necessarily makes him a caller to his innovation.
C) Because the hadith imams agreed that all innovators are callers.
D) Because the distinction is not mentioned in any primary source.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position, supported by rational necessity and caution, is that all narrations from innovators are rejected because every innovator is necessarily a caller to his innovation.

Innovators Who Permit Lying: Unconditional Rejection
Classification and ruling on narrations of innovators who permit lying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two categories of innovators with respect to lying and state the Hanafi ruling that the narrations of those who permit lying are absolutely rejected.

Core Idea
If an innovator permits lying in his religion, his narration is absolutely rejected without further scrutiny.

In the previous discussion, Hanafis differed about accepting narrations from innovators who do not permit lying. But a separate category exists: those who consider lying permissible in their religion. This lesson classifies such groups and establishes the absolute rejection of their narrations, supported by evidence from practice and the principle of precaution in matters of religion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of innovators
The author distinguishes between innovators who do not permit lying (subject to dispute) and those who do permit it (absolutely rejected). List the latter: Karamiyya, Ghulat Rafidah, Imamiyyah.
The distinction hinges on whether lying is considered permissible in their sect.
2
Analyze the legal definition of 'innovator who permits lying'
Using the legal_definition_dissector, identify the hukm (rejection), mahall (the groups), sabab (permission of lying), and athar (no reliance on their narrations).
3
Examine the supporting evidence
The author provides an anecdote about a Rafidi doctor's treatise permitting hadith fabrication. Also cites Ibn Sirin's statement: 'The Sunan are your religion, so look from whom you take your religion.'
These illustrate the practical necessity of rejecting such narrators.
4
Apply the rule to testimony (shahada) vs. narration (riwaya)
The author notes that even testimony from such innovators is rejected (e.g., Khattabiyya), but narration is even more strict because it involves the Prophet's words.
Testimony is affected by tuhma (accusation of bias); narration is pure transmission.
5
State the Hanafi position and its basis
The Hanafi school unanimously rejects narrations from those who permit lying, because their religiosity does not prevent them from fabricating hadith.
This is a definitive ruling, not subject to ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ظناً أنه نسب في تلك الرسائل هذا القول إلى الإمام علي ابن موسى الرضا قدس سره وأسرار آبائه الكرام، وهذا كذب آخر صدر منهم، وما قيل: إن البعض قبلوا بعض المتشيعين فليس هؤلاء المتشيعون من الإمامية والغلاة والخصومة، وهي تفضي إلى الوضع وأيضاً مذهبهم هو القيام على هواهم وعدالتهم تصلبهم على الهوى والتعصب والغلو، فعدالتهم موقعة في شبهة الوضع ومبني أمر السنة على الاحتياط، ألم تر أن الإمام الهمام أبا حنيفة منهم
Then know that the dispute is about the people of innovation who do not permit lying. As for those who permit it, like the Karamiyya, their narration is absolutely not accepted, because since lying is permissible in their religion in their belief, they do not care about committing it. Among them are the Ghulat Rafidah (extremist Shia) and the Imamiyyah. Indeed, lying is most apparent and famous among them, to the point that they have become a byword for lying. They have permitted committing all sins, even disbelief, under taqiyyah when they know the anger and displeasure of those who know their sect. Rather, they obligate sins in this situation, so there is no safety that they will lie about the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace and his Companions, in this situation, and they do not care about lying about the Messenger of Allah and his Companions. Whoever looks at their books finds that most of the transmitted reports are fabricated and forged; their wording and meaning testify to their forgery. I heard some reliable person say: The reliable Muhammad Tahir al-Shanburi narrated to me: I was occupied with medicine under the doctor named Shukr Allah al-Sindilawi, and he was a wicked Rafidi. He had a volume containing treatises on their essential beliefs, and he used to hide it from me. He was a most vehement disputant, arguing with me every day and calling me to his desire. One day I managed to take it in his absence, and I took it and read it. I found in some of those treatises the permissibility of fabricating hadith when disputing with Ahl al-Sunnah. Then when he wanted to argue with me another day, I said: 'Avoid argument and fear Allah, for in the school of your likes, fabricating hadith is permissible.' He said, 'From where do you say that?' I said, 'From your treatises.' So he was confounded, the one who transgressed. And this servant recalls, thinking that in those treatises this statement was attributed to Imam 'Ali ibn Musa al-Rida (may Allah sanctify his secret) and the secrets of his noble forefathers. This is another lie from them. And what is said that some accepted some of the Tashayyu' (preferring 'Ali) is not about these Imamiyyah and Ghulat, but about those who merely prefer 'Ali over the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and 'Umar), as clarified by Ibn Taymiyyah and others. So understand and be firm: [The statement] 'that his religiosity prevents him from lying' means that the people of desire have not left the fold of Islam due to their desire, so their religiosity with this religion prevents them from lying because it is forbidden in their religion. The discussion about 'adala in his school concerns one who does not make lying religiously permissible. Hence, the testimony of ahl al-ahwa is accepted absolutely (except for al-Khattabiyya). They are from the Ghulat Rafidah, followers of Abu al-Khattab. Their school includes the permissibility of testimony for one who swears by them, due to their claim that a Muslim does not swear falsely. It is also said that their school includes permissibility of testimony for their own kind. So their testimony is not accepted due to these two accusations, but narration is not like that, as there is no suspicion in it at all. Thus it is said: First, it is refuted by the example of the disbeliever: lying is forbidden in their religion, and their religiosity with their false religion prevents them from lying, since the discussion is about 'adala in one's religion, so the disbeliever should also be accepted. If you say, 'They have no authority over us,' I say, 'Narration is not a matter of authority.' Second, the solution is that his religion does not prevent him from lying absolutely, but only from lying that does not harm his desire. This is because the bulk of his religion is desire and whim, and he is not guided by it to the straight path. Rather, this religion prevents him from abandoning his desire that he is upon, and his desire impels him to argument and dispute, which leads to fabrication. Also, their school is to uphold their desires, and their 'adala makes them firm upon desire, bigotry, and extremism. So their 'adala is placed in suspicion of fabrication, and the matter of the Sunnah is built upon precaution. Have you not seen that the great Imam Abu Hanifah disliked following the disputatious theologian even if he was upon the truth? And the verifying scholars turned away from the disputatious theologian in taking hadith, until some gnostics said that I did not write many hadiths because their narrators were theologians. If such is their condition, then what is the condition of the people of innovation?
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a classification and discussion of innovators with respect to their permissibility of lying. The author says the previous dispute (about acceptance of narrations) applies only to innovators who do not permit lying. As for those who do permit it, such as the Karamiyya (a theological sect known for anthropomorphism), their narrations are absolutely unacceptable because they consider lying permissible, so they do not care about committing it. Among these are the Ghulat Rafidah (extremist Shia) and the Imamiyyah (Twelver Shia). The author asserts that lying is famous among them, and they permit all sins, even disbelief, under the doctrine of taqiyyah when they fear harm from those who know their sect. In fact, they obligate sins in such situations, so they would not hesitate to lie about the Prophet (peace be upon him) and his Companions. He provides a personal anecdote about a Rafidi doctor who had a treatise permitting hadith fabrication during disputes with Ahl al-Sunnah, and when confronted, the man was confounded, and the treatise attributed this view to Imam 'Ali al-Rida (which the author considers another lie). The author then clarifies that the term 'mutashayyi' (one who prefers 'Ali) often used by some scholars does not refer to these Ghulat but to those who merely prefer 'Ali over the two shaykhs, as Ibn Taymiyyah stated. He then returns to the earlier rebuttal of the argument that 'his religiosity prevents him from lying.' This argument was used to justify accepting testimony from ahl al-ahwa. The author refutes it with two points: (1) It is refuted by the example of the disbeliever, who also considers lying forbidden in his religion but is not accepted because he lacks 'adala in our religion. (2) In reality, the innovator's religion does not prevent him from lying absolutely; it only prevents him from lying that harms his desire, because his core is desire (hawa), and his religion actually encourages him to argue and dispute, leading to fabrication. Furthermore, their very 'adala (moral probity) is suspect because it makes them steadfast upon desire, bigotry, and extremism. Therefore, the Sunnah is a matter of precaution. The author cites Imam Abu Hanifah's dislike of following a disputatious theologian even if he is upon the truth, and the practice of verifying scholars who avoided taking hadith from such theologians. Thus, all the more so for innovators.

Key Terms
الكرامية (al-Karamiyya): A theological sect following Muhammad ibn Karram, known for anthropomorphic views and permissibility of lying for sectarian benefit.
الروافض الغلاة (al-rawafid al-ghulat): Extremist Shia groups who reject the first two caliphs and permit lying, often associated with tabarra' and taqiyyah.
التقية (al-taqiyyah): Dissimulation of one's faith when under threat; the Rafidah use it to permit lying and even disbelief in fear of harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul presented in this lesson, why are the narrations of the Karamiyya absolutely rejected?
A) Because they are a theological sect.
B) Because they permit lying in their religion.
C) Because they are Shia.
D) Because they are known individually as liars.

Key Takeaway
Innovators who permit lying in their religion have their narrations absolutely rejected because their doctrine allows fabrication, making them unreliable transmitters of the Sunnah.

Refuting the Argument: Religiosity Does Not Prevent Lying
Critical refutation of the argument that religiosity prevents lying, applied to innovators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi critique of the argument that an innovator's religiosity prevents him from lying, and apply the distinction between the disbeliever and the innovator in issues of 'adala.

Core Idea
The innovator's religion is driven by hawa, so it prevents only lies that harm his desire, not lies that serve his sect.

After establishing the classification of innovators, the author addresses a counterargument: some claim that even innovators who permit lying might be restrained by their religiosity. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi refutation of that argument, showing why it fails and how the principle of precaution in the Sunnah demands absolute rejection of their narrations.

Guided Path
1
State the argument under critique
The argument is: the innovator's religiosity (din) prevents him from lying because lying is forbidden in his religion. So his narration should be accepted.
This argument is often used to defend accepting testimony from ahl al-ahwa.
2
Identify the first rebuttal: the disbeliever analogy
The author points out that if we accept that reasoning, we would also have to accept the disbeliever's narration, since lying is forbidden in his religion and his religiosity should prevent lying. Yet we reject the disbeliever because his religion is false.
The difference: the disbeliever lacks 'adala in Islamic law; the innovator is still within Islam.
3
Analyze the second and deeper rebuttal
The innovator's religion is based on hawa (desire). His religiosity only prevents lies that harm his desire, not lies that protect or promote his sect. In fact, his 'adala reinforces his bigotry and makes him more likely to fabricate.
The author uses the analogy of Imam Abu Hanifah disliking theologians even if they are correct.
4
Apply the principle of ihtiyat (precaution)
Since the Sunnah is the foundation of religion, we must be extremely cautious. Even if there is a possibility of fabrication, we reject the narration. The verifying scholars avoided taking hadith from disputatious theologians.
Caution overrides any potential benefit from accepting such narrations.
5
Conclude the Hanafi position
Thus, the argument that 'religiosity prevents lying' is invalid for innovators who permit lying because their 'adala is subservient to desire. Their narrations are absolutely rejected.
This also applies to those who do not permit lying but are driven by hawa; they are subject to dispute but caution is recommended.

دينه لا يصده عن الكذب مطلقاً، بل عن الكذب الذي لا يضره هواه، وهذا لأن جل دينه الهوى والشرارة لا يهتدي به إلى سواء السبيل، بل دينه هذا يصده عن الخروج عن هواه الذي هو عليه وهواه الذي يحرضه على المجادلة والخصومة، وهي تفضي إلى الوضع وأيضاً مذهبهم هو القيام على هواهم وعدالتهم تصلبهم على الهوى والتعصب والغلو، فعدالتهم موقعة في شبهة الوضع ومبني أمر السنة على الاحتياط، ألم تر أن الإمام الهمام أبا حنيفة Key Terms
الهوى (al-hawa): Desire or whimsical inclination; in this context, the driving force behind the innovator's religion that overrides moral considerations.
الخطابية (al-Khattabiyya): A Ghulat Shia sect following Abu al-Khattab, known for permitting false testimony for their members.

According to the passage, what is the fundamental flaw in the argument that an innovator's religiosity prevents him from lying?

Rejection of Narrations from the Rafidah due to Permissibility of Lying
Critique of Rafidah narrators due to permissible lying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion of 'adalah to evaluate narrators from sects that permit lying, using the Rafidah as a case study.

Core Idea
A narrator whose doctrine permits lying forfeits 'adalah — his narrations are rejected due to suspicion of fabrication.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut explains why the Hanafi school rejects narrations from the Rafidah (Imamiyyah). The central argument is that their doctrine of taqiyyah permits lying, even fabricating hadith, to promote their sect. The commentary refutes the counterargument that religiosity prevents lying, showing that for ahl al-ahwa, desire overrides religion. By understanding this reasoning, you will grasp a key principle in Hanafi usul: the requirement of 'adalah in narrators goes beyond surface religiosity and examines doctrinal stances on major sins.

Guided Path
1
Identify the narrator category under critique
Locate the term 'Rafidah' and its synonym 'Imamiyyah' in the passage. Understand that these are extreme Shi'a who reject the first two caliphs and are known for permitting lying under taqiyyah.
The passage distinguishes between extreme Rafidah and those who merely prefer 'Ali over the two shaykhs (mutashayyi'un). The latter are not automatically rejected.
2
Identify the specific flaw: permissibility of lying
Read the description: they permit all sins, including lying and fabricating hadith, when disputing with Ahl al-Sunnah. This is not just individual sin but official doctrine.
The story of the Rafidi doctor illustrates this: the treatises explicitly permit hadith fabrication in debate.
3
Analyze the counterargument and its refutation
The text presents an opposing view: 'his religiosity prevents him from lying.' The Hanafi response clarifies: their religion is subservient to desire; they only avoid lies that harm their party, not lies that advance it.
The response also uses a reductio: if religiosity prevented lying, even disbelievers would be accepted, but they are not because their 'false religion' does not restrain them from lying about Islam.
4
Apply the principle of caution in accepting hadith
The passage invokes Imam Abu Hanifah's dislike of speculative theologians (mutakallimin) as narrators, even if they are on truth. This reinforces the need for extra caution when dealing with those engaged in sectarian debate.
The conclusion: the Sunnah is based on caution, so narrations from groups that permit lying are rejected outright.
5
Connect to the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Contrast the Hanafi position with schools that accept narrations from ahl al-ahwa if they appear truthful. In practice, Hanafis do not accept hadith from known Rafidi narrators, while others may evaluate individual trustworthiness.
This difference leads to different hadith acceptance lists across madhhabs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منهم والإمامية، فإن الكذب فيهم أظهر وأشهر حتى صار واضرب المثل في الكذب، وهم جوّزوا ارتكاب جميع المعاصي حتى الكفر تقية عند معرفتهم غضب من عرف بمذهبهم وسخطه عليهم، بل يوجبون المعاصي في هذه الحال، فلا أمان أن يكذبوا على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم عند هذه الحال، وهم لا يبالون بالكذب على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم ومن نظر في كتبهم لم يجد أكثر المرويات إلا موضوعة مفتراة يشهد على كونها مفتراة عبارتها ومفادها، وقد سمعت بعض الثقات يقول ؛ حدثني الثقة محمد طاهر الشانبوري أني كنت مشتغلاً بالطب عند الطبيب المسمى بشكر الله السنديلوى وكان رافضياً خبيثاً، وكان عنده مجلد فيه رسائل مشتملة على عقائدهم الضرورية، وكان يخبئها مني، وكان مخاصماً ألد الخصام، يجادلني كل يوم ويدعوني إلى هواه، فقدرت يوماً على أخذ ذلك عند غيبته، فأخذته فطالعته، فإذا وجدت في بعض تلك الرسائل جواز وضع الحديث عند المخاصمة مع أهل السنة، ثم إذا أراد يوماً آخر المجادلة إياي قلت : إياك والمجادلة واتق الله ، فإن في مذهب أمثالك جواز وضع الأحاديث، فقال: من أين تقول ؟ قلت: من رسائلك، فبهت الذي فسق ويتذكر هذا العبد ظناً أنه نسب في تلك الرسائل هذا القول إلى الإمام علي ابن موسى الرضا قدس سره وأسرار آبائه الكرام، وهذا كذب آخر صدر منهم، وما قيل: إن البعض قبلوا بعض المتشيعين فليس هؤلاء المتشيعون من الإمامية والغلاة بل هؤلاء المتشيعة الراؤون تفضيل أمير المؤمنين علي كرّم الله وجهه على الشيخين صرح به ابن تيمية وغيره فافهم وتثبت لنا أن تدينه يصده عن الكذب) يعني أن أهل الهوى لم يخرج بهواه عن ملة الإسلام، فتدينه بهذا الدين يصده عن الكذب لكونه محرم دينه والكلام في العدل في مذهبه فيمن لا يتدين يحل الكذب ومن ههنا قبل شهادة أهل الأهواء مطلقاً (إلا الخطابية) هم من غلاة الروافض تابعون لأبي الخطاب، ومن مذهبهم حل الشهادة لمن يحلف عندهم، لزعمهم أن المسلم لا يحلف كاذباً، وقيل من مذهبهم حل الشهادة لأهل جليدتهم، فلا تقبل شهادتهم لأجل هذين التهمتين، ولا كذلك الرواية، إذ لا شبهة فيه أصلاً، كذا قيل : أولاً: إنه منقوض بالكافر فإن الكذب محرم في دينهم وتدينهم بدينهم الباطل يصدهم عن الكذب، إذ الكلام في العدل في دينه فينبغي أن يقبل الكافر أيضاً، فإن قلت لا ولاية لهم علينا، قلت: الرواية ليست من باب الولاية : وثانياً الحل أن دينه لا يصده عن الكذب مطلقاً، بل عن الكذب الذي لا يضره ،هواه، وهذا لأن جل دينه ،الهوى والشرارة لا يهتدي به إلى سواء السبيل، بل دينه هذا يصده عن الخروج عن هواه الذي هو عليه وهواه الذي يحرضه على المجادلة والخصومة، وهي تفضي إلى الوضع وأيضاً مذهبهم هو القيام على هواهم وعدالتهم تصلبهم على الهوى والتعصب والغلو، فعدالتهم موقعة في شبهة الوضع ومبني أمر السنة على الاحتياط، ألم تر أن الإمام الهمام أبا حنيفة كره الاقتداء بالمتكلم المجادل ولو كان على الحق، والمحققون أعرضوا عن المتكلم المجادل في أخذ الحديث، حتى قال بعض العرفاء ما كتبت الأحاديث كثيرة لكون رواتها متكلمين، فإذا كان حالهم هذا فما حال أهل البدع منهم (ولقوله ) معطوف
Among them are the Imamiyyah (Rafidah), for lying is most apparent and famous among them until a proverb was made about lying. They permitted committing all sins, even disbelief, as taqiyyah when they know the anger and displeasure of those who know their sect. Rather, they obligate sins in this situation, so there is no safety from them lying about the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings upon him and his family and companions) in this situation. They do not care about lying about the Messenger of Allah. He who looks at their books finds most narrations except fabricated and forged, their expression and meaning testify to their being forged. I heard some trustworthy people say: The trustworthy Muhammad Tahir al-Shanburi narrated to me that I was studying medicine under the doctor named Shukr Allah al-Sindilawi, and he was a wretched Rafidi. He had a volume containing treatises comprising their essential beliefs, which he would hide from me. He was a vehement disputant, arguing with me every day and calling me to his desires. One day I managed to take it in his absence, so I took it and examined it. I found in some of those treatises the permissibility of fabricating hadith when disputing with Ahl al-Sunnah. Then when he wanted to debate me another day, I said: Avoid debate and fear Allah, for in the school of your likes is the permissibility of fabricating hadith. He said: From where do you say that? I said: From your treatises. Then he who transgressed was dumbfounded. And this slave thinks that he attributed this statement to Imam Ali ibn Musa al-Rida (may Allah sanctify his secret and the secrets of his noble forefathers) in those treatises. This is another lie from them. And what is said: that some accepted some of the mutashayyi'un (those inclined to Shi'ism) — these mutashayyi'un are not from the Imamiyyah and the extremists, but rather these are those who prefer Amir al-Mu'minin Ali (may Allah honor his face) over the two shaykhs, as stated by Ibn Taymiyyah and others. So understand and be certain. Then the statement: 'for his religiosity prevents him from lying' means that Ahl al-Ahwa (people of desires) have not left the religion of Islam due to their desire. So his religiosity with this religion prevents him from lying because it is forbidden in his religion. The discussion is about 'adalah in his school regarding one who does not practice religion that permits lying. Hence, the testimony of Ahl al-Ahwa is accepted absolutely, except the Khattabiyyah. They are from the Ghulat of the Rafidah, followers of Abu al-Khattab. Their school permits testimony for one who swears by them, due to their claim that a Muslim does not swear falsely. It is said that their school permits testimony for their own group. So their testimony is not accepted for these two accusations. But this does not apply to narration, for there is no such suspicion at all. Thus it is said: First, it is refuted by the disbeliever, for lying is forbidden in their religion and their religiosity with their false religion prevents them from lying, so the discussion is about 'adalah in his religion. Therefore, the disbeliever should also be accepted. If you say: they have no authority over us, I say: narration is not a matter of authority. Second, the solution is that his religion does not prevent him from lying absolutely, but rather from lying that does not harm his desire. This is because most of his religion is desire and evil, which does not guide him to the straight path. Rather, this religion of his prevents him from going against his desire, and his desire incites him to argument and disputation, which leads to fabrication. Also, their school is to uphold their desires, and their 'adalah makes them steadfast in desire, partisanship, and extremism. So their 'adalah is subject to suspicion of fabrication, and the matter of the Sunnah is built on caution. Do you not see that the great Imam Abu Hanifah disliked following the speculative theologian who argues, even if he is upon the truth? And the investigators turned away from the speculative theologian who argues in taking hadith, until some of the gnostics said: I did not write many hadith because their narrators were speculative theologians. If this is their state, then what is the state of the people of innovation from them? (And due to his statement) is attached.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi commentary discussing the unreliability of narrators from the Rafidah (Imamiyyah Shi'a). The author argues that their doctrine permits lying under taqiyyah, even fabricating hadith when disputing with Ahl al-Sunnah, as illustrated by a story about a Rafidi doctor. He distinguishes between extreme Rafidah and those who merely prefer Ali over the two shaykhs (mutashayyi'un). The commentary then examines the counterargument that a person's religiosity prevents them from lying, but refutes it: for ahl al-ahwa, their desire overrides their religion, so they may lie to advance their sect. The only exception mentioned is the Khattabiyyah, a group of extreme Rafidah, whose testimony is rejected due to specific accusations. The passage concludes by invoking Imam Abu Hanifah's dislike of speculative theologians (mutakallimin) as narrators, reinforcing the caution needed when accepting hadith from those engaged in sectarian debate.

Key Terms
رافضي (Rafidi): An extreme Shi'a who rejects the caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar; here, specifically the Imamiyyah who permit lying in taqiyyah.
تقية (Taqiyyah): Dissimulation of one's faith when under threat; the Rafidah extend this to permit lying and even fabrication of hadith in disputation.
عدالة ('Adalah): Moral integrity required for a narrator's acceptance; here, compromised by permissible lying and sectarian bias.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are narrations from the Rafidah (Imamiyyah) rejected according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because they are not Muslims
B) Because their doctrine permits lying and fabricating hadith in sectarian disputes
C) Because they do not pray or fast
D) Because they are all ignorant

Key Takeaway
A narrator from a group that permits lying in sectarian disputes lacks 'adalah, regardless of his personal conduct.

Conditions for Accepting Hadith al-Wahid
Conditions for accepting khabar al-wahid: number, freedom, gender, sight, qadhf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the conditions that are NOT required for accepting khabar al-wahid according to the Hanafi school and explain the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
One just narrator suffices for khabar al-wahid; the strict conditions of testimony do not apply.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut clarifies the Hanafi position on the acceptance of single-narrator hadith (khabar al-wahid). Unlike testimony, which requires multiple strict conditions, narrating hadith is more flexible. The author contrasts the Hanafi view with that of the Mu'tazila and explains why freedom, male gender, and sight are not required, drawing on the practice of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main issue
The passage discusses whether conditions like number of narrators, freedom, male gender, and sight are required for accepting a khabar al-wahid. Note the Hanafi position that they are not.
2
Understand the opposing view
Al-Jubba'i (Mu'tazila) argues that number is required, analogizing to testimony where multiple witnesses are needed. The Hanafis reject this analogy.
The Hanafis point out that requiring number in testimony is itself an exception to qiyas due to forgery.
3
Analyze the Hanafi evidence
The Hanafis cite the practice of 'Umar who initially rejected Abu Musa's hadith but accepted it when a second narrator corroborated, showing number is not a condition for truth but for certainty. General proofs for the authority of khabar al-wahid also apply.
The dalil is from Sunnah ahad (practice of Companions).
4
Compare with testimony
Freedom, male gender, and sight are required for testimony but not for narration. The Hanafis base this on the practice of the Companions who accepted reports from slaves (Burayrah), women (Aisha, Umm Salamah), and blind persons (Ibn Umm Maktum, Ibn Abbas).
Kinship and enmity also do not disqualify a narrator.
5
Examine the case of the qadhf narrator
A person flogged for false accusation of adultery (qadhf) is accepted as a narrator after repentance. This is the relied-upon view, though a weak riwaya from Abu Hanifa rejects it by analogy to testimony.
The text considers the rejection as 'khilaf al-zahir'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العدد ليس بشرط في الرواية عند الجمهور خلافاً للجبائي من المعتزلة فإنه يقال : لا بد من عدد الشهادة حتى أوجب في أحاديث الزنا أربعة رووها إلا إذا أيد مؤيد فلا يحتاج حينئذ إلى العدد، بل يكفي الواحد وقد تقدم المأخذ من قبل، فإنه يستدل برد أمير المؤمنين عمر خبر أبي موسى الأشعري في الاستئذان حتى رواه أبو سعيد ولنا ما تقدم من الدلائل على حجية خبر الواحد، فإنها غير فارقة وربما يقيس على الشهادة وهو غير سديد فإن اشتراط العدد فيها على خلاف القياس لكثرة التزوير فيها ولا الحرية شرطاً، بخلاف الشهادة إذ لا بد فيها من الولاية ولا الذكورة فيقبل خبر المرأة العادلة من غير مشاركة رجل معها، بخلاف الشهادة إذ اشتراط الذكورة فيها بالنص على خلاف القياس ولا البصر بخلاف الشهادة، فإن أمرها أضيق، وإنما لم تشترط الحرية والذكورة والبصر اقتداء بالصحابة رضوان الله تعالى عليهم، وكفى بهم قدوة، وهم قد قبلوا خبر بريرة قبل العتاق وخبر عائشة الصديقة أم المؤمنين، وأم المؤمنين أم سلمة ،وغيرهما، وخبر عبد الله ابن أم مكتوم وابن عباس بعد ابتلائه بذهاب البصر ولا عدم القرابة بين الراوي وبين من ينفعه مضمون الحديث ولا عدم العداوة بينه وبين من يضره العموم الحديث في حق الكل، حتى يلزم الراوي والمروي له وغيرهما، فلا تهمة، بخلاف الشهادة، فإنها مختصة بالمشهود له والمشهود عليه نفعاً وضراً ولا الحد في قذف فإنه تقبل رواية المحدود في القذف بعد التوبة وروي عن الإمام أبي حنيفة في رواية الحسن خلافه أي عدم القبول وإن تاب قياساً على الشهادة وهو خلاف الظاهر من عدم
Then the number is not a condition in narration according to the majority, contrary to al-Jubba'i from the Mu'tazila who say: the number required for testimony is necessary, so they require four narrators for hadiths about adultery unless there is a supporting evidence, then even one suffices. The reasoning is from when Umar rejected Abu Musa al-Ash'ari's hadith on seeking permission until Abu Sa'id narrated it. Our proof is the previous evidence for the authority of khabar al-wahid, which does not distinguish; one might analogize to testimony but that is unsound because requiring number there is contrary to analogy due to frequent forgery. Freedom is not a condition, unlike testimony where guardianship is necessary. Male gender is not a condition: a just woman's report is accepted without a man's participation, unlike testimony where male gender is required by text contrary to analogy. Sight is not a condition, unlike testimony where matters are stricter. These (freedom, male gender, sight) were not made conditions in emulation of the Companions, sufficient as exemplars; they accepted the report of Burayrah before emancipation, the report of Aisha, Umm Salamah, and others, and the report of Abdullah ibn Umm Maktum and Ibn Abbas after he became blind. The lack of kinship between the narrator and one whom the hadith benefits, and the lack of enmity between him and one whom it harms, are not conditions because the hadith applies to all, so the narrator is bound by it, thus no suspicion, unlike testimony which is specific to the litigants for benefit or harm. Regarding the punishment for qadhf, the narration of one who has been flogged for qadhf is accepted after repentance. It is narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa in the narration of al-Hasan that he disagreed, i.e., non-acceptance even after repentance by analogy to testimony, but this is contrary to the apparent from?
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the conditions for accepting a report from a single narrator (khabar al-wahid). The author states that unlike testimony (shahada), khabar al-wahid does not require a specific number of narrators; one just person suffices. This is the view of the majority (jumuhr) including the Hanafis, as opposed to the Mu'tazila al-Jubba'i who demand multiple narrators like for testimony. The Hanafi proof includes the practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab who initially rejected Abu Musa's hadith on seeking permission but accepted it when another narrator (Abu Sa'id) supported it, showing that number is not a prerequisite for acceptance in principle. Furthermore, analogy to testimony is rejected because the requirement of number in testimony is a special ruling (khilaf al-qiyas) due to the high risk of forgery. Then the author enumerates other conditions that are NOT required for narration: freedom (al-hurriyya), male gender (al-dhukura), and sight (al-basr), all of which are required for testimony. The justification is the practice of the Companions who accepted reports from a slave (Burayrah before emancipation), women (Aisha, Umm Salamah), and blind persons (Abdullah ibn Umm Maktum, Ibn Abbas). Similarly, kinship or enmity do not disqualify a narrator because the hadith binds all, unlike testimony which is only for litigants. Finally, the author addresses the acceptance of a narrator who has been punished for qadhf (false accusation of adultery). After repentance, his narration is accepted. This is the relied-upon position. There is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa (in a riwaya from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad) that he rejected it even after repentance, analogizing to testimony. But the text considers this position weak (khilaf al-zahir).

Key Terms
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A report transmitted by one just narrator, establishing speculative knowledge (zann) but binding for action.
المحدود في القذف (al-mahdud fi al-qadhf): A person who has been flogged for false accusation of adultery; his narration is accepted after repentance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for accepting khabar al-wahid according to the majority (including Hanafis)?
A) The narrator must be just.
B) The narrator must be free.
C) The narrator must be male.
D) The narrator must be of sound mind.

Key Takeaway
Khabar al-wahid from a single just narrator is accepted without requiring number, freedom, male gender, or sight, unlike testimony.

Knowledge of Meaning: A Condition for Narrator Acceptance?
Debate on requiring knowledge of meaning for narrator acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement of knowledge of meaning for narrator acceptance, evaluate the opposing proof, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Knowledge of meaning is required for narrator acceptance because the intent of hadith is meaning, not mere words.

This passage addresses a critical usul dispute: must a hadith narrator understand the meaning of what he transmits? The Hanafi school answers yes, while some scholars hold that integrity alone suffices. The author presents the Hanafi proof from the nature of preservation, then rebuts the opponent's key evidence—the hadith 'نضر الله امرأ'. Understanding this reasoning sharpens your ability to evaluate narrators and proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed condition
What exactly is being debated? It is knowledge of linguistic meaning (not legal meaning). The author clarifies the dispute is about linguistic understanding, not fiqh.
Note the distinction: the condition is 'ilm al-ma'na al-lughawi, not 'ilm al-ma'na al-shar'i.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi school requires knowledge of meaning. Their proof: the purpose of hadith is meaning; preservation requires understanding; analogy to Quran memorization.
Key phrase in text: 'وعندنا يشترط الثالث' and 'المقصود في الحديث هو المعنى'.
3
State the opposing position and its dalil
Opponents argue it is not required. Their evidence: the hadith 'نضر الله امرأ' which implies merely hearing and retaining suffices.
The hadith is cited in al-Mukhtasar.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response: that hadith is a supplication, not a legal condition; the narrator's lack of understanding still creates suspicion of error, so authority is not established.
The author says: 'الحديث إنما هو دعاء للحافظ الراوي، ولا يلزم منه قبول السامع إياه'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: whether the narration of a non-faqih who is otherwise upright and precise is accepted. Hanafis reject it; others accept it.
This shows the real-world impact of the usul dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثاني السنة
المذهب لقبول أبي بكرة فإنه قذف المغيرة بن شعبة فحده أمير المؤمنين عمر، وحسان ومسطح بن أثاثة كونهما محدودين حين قذفا عائشة الصديقة فبرأها الله تعالى وكذبهما الله مع حين افترى عبد الله بن أبي المنافق، لكنهم تابوا عن هذا الأمر الشنيع (ولا الإكثار من الرواية) كيف وإن زبيراً رضي الله تعالى عنه لم يكثر رواية الحديث (ولا) معرفة النسب) كما اشتراط بعض أهل الحديث إذ العدالة هي السبب لعدم الكذب وعلة الظن بالصدق، ولا دخل فيه للنسب ولا علم الفقه أو العربية أو معنى الحديث (نعم) هما (أولى) وعندنا يشترط الثالث، وليس الخلاف في العلم بالمعاني الشرعية وعلة الحكم، وإنما النزاع في المعنى اللغوي لنا: أن المقصود في الحديث هو المعنى، ولا يتصدى للضبط إلا من له معرفة بالمعنى عادة، ألم تر لم يتصد أحد للكتب بقراءة متن الحديث من غير معرفة المعنى، كما يشاهد في تعلم القرآن الشريف، ففي نقل من لا دراية له في فهم المعنى ريبة التساهل وعدم ضبط حقه، حتى لو علم رجل له تصد لضبط المتن مثل التصدي لضبط متن القرآن تقبل ،روايته، لكنه لم يوجد بعد، ومن ههنا ظهر فساد القياس على نقل القرآن فافهم واستدل في «المختصر) على عدم اشتراط العلم بالمعنى بقوله صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه وسلم: «نضر») بالتخفيف في رواية أبي عبيد، وقال وهو متعد ولازم وبالتشديد فيما قال الأصمعي وقال : المخفف لازم، والتشديد للتعدية، وعلى الأول للمبالغة والتكثير النضر والنضارة في الأصل حسن الوجه والبريق .
والمراد ههنا رفع القدر والمرتبة، كذا قال الشيخ عبد الحق رحمه الله تعالى: «الله أمر أسمع مني حديثاً فوعى فرواه كما وعى فرب حامل فقه غير فقيه، ورب حامل فقه إلى من هو أفقه منه رواه أبو داود ورواه الترمذي والشافعي والبيهقي (أقول : هذا دعاء للصادق في الرواية عدلاً ضابطاً أولا، والمقصود ههنا (تحصيل ضابطة للسامع) ليفيد غلبة ظن الصدق (دفعاً للريبة ريبة تعمد الكذب والنسيان (فالاستلزام) للمطلوب (ممنوع) والحاصل أن الحديث إنما هو دعاء للحافظ الراوي، ولا يلزم منه قبول السامع إياه، ووجوب العمل عليه بمقتضاه، كيف وإذا كان غير عالم بالمعنى فريبة التساهل وسوء الضبط باقية ومعهما لا ظن للصدق فلا حجية (فتدبر) وربما يستدلون بأن مبنى القبول على العدالة وهي موجودة، وقد اندفع بما مر فتذكر
The second principle: the Sunnah. The madhhab accepts Abu Bakra because he accused al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba, and the Commander of the Faithful Umar had him flogged; and Hassan and Mistah ibn Uthatha because they were flogged when they accused Aisha the truthful, but Allah cleared her and discredited them, along with when Abdullah ibn Ubayy the hypocrite fabricated [lies], but they repented from this heinous act. (And not by abundance of narration) how? Ibn Zubair, may Allah be pleased with him, did not narrate many hadith. (And not by knowledge of lineage) as some hadith scholars stipulate, for integrity is the cause for the absence of lying and the reasoning for the presumption of truthfulness, and lineage has no part in it, nor knowledge of fiqh, Arabic, or meaning of hadith. (Yes) both are (better). With us, the third is required. The disagreement is not about knowledge of legal meanings and the rationale of the ruling, but rather the dispute is about the linguistic meaning. For us: The intended [thing] in the hadith is the meaning, and no one undertakes preservation except one who normally has knowledge of the meaning. Have you not seen that no one undertakes reading books of hadith texts without knowledge of the meaning, as is observed in learning the Noble Quran. So in the transmission of one who has no discernment in understanding the meaning, there is suspicion of carelessness and lack of proper preservation. Even if a man were seen who undertakes to preserve the text like undertaking to preserve the Quran text, his narration would be accepted, but he has not yet existed. From this, the invalidity of analogy on Quran transmission appears, so understand. And he adduced in al-Mukhtasar for not requiring knowledge of the meaning by his statement, peace be upon him: 'نضر' with lightening in Abu Ubayd's narration, and he said it is both transitive and intransitive, and with shaddah according to al-Asma'i, and he said: the lightened is intransitive, the shaddah is for transitivity, and the first is for exaggeration and multiplicity. Al-Nadr and al-nadara originally mean beauty of face and luster. And what is intended here is elevation of status and rank. Thus said Shaykh Abd al-Haqq, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Allah commanded: whoever hears a hadith from me and preserves it and narrates it as he preserved it, for perhaps a bearer of fiqh is not a faqih, and perhaps a bearer of fiqh conveys it to one who is more learned than him.' Narrated by Abu Dawud, and narrated by al-Tirmidhi, al-Shafi'i, and al-Bayhaqi. I say: This is a supplication for the truthful narrator who is upright and accurate first, and what is intended here is (acquiring a criterion for the listener) to produce a preponderance of presumption of truthfulness (to repel suspicion, suspicion of intentional lying and forgetfulness). So the entailment of the intended is prevented. The summary is that the hadith is only a supplication for the preserving narrator, and it does not necessitate the listener accepting him and the obligation to act according to it. How? If he is not knowledgeable of the meaning, the suspicion of carelessness and poor preservation remains, and with them there is no presumption of truthfulness, so there is no authority. (So reflect). And perhaps they infer that the basis of acceptance is integrity, and it is present, and that has been repelled by what has passed, so remember.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute about whether a hadith narrator must have knowledge of the meaning (i.e., fiqh or understanding) of the hadith. The author begins by listing the conditions for accepting a narrator: integrity (adalah), precision (dabt), and then addresses knowledge of meaning. Some scholars stipulate knowledge of lineage or language, but the author asserts that only integrity is the cause for trustworthiness; knowledge of meaning is not a condition according to some, but it is preferable. However, the Hanafi school (indicated by 'عندنا') requires the third condition—knowledge of the meaning. The dispute is not about legal understanding but about linguistic meaning. The proof: the purpose of hadith is its meaning, and only one who understands the meaning can properly preserve it, as seen with Quran memorization. The opponent argues from the hadith 'نضر الله امرأ...' that a narrator only needs to hear and convey. The author rebuts that this hadith is a supplication for the truthful narrator, not a condition for acceptance, and that without understanding, suspicion of error remains. Thus, knowledge of meaning is essential for authority.

Key Terms
عدالة (adalah): Integrity of the narrator, making him refrain from lying and fulfilling religious obligations
ضبط (dabt): Precision and accuracy in preserving and transmitting the hadith
فقيه (faqih): A person who has deep understanding of legal rulings and the meanings of texts

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi position on knowledge of meaning for a hadith narrator?
A) It is required as a condition for acceptance.
B) It is not required; only integrity matters.
C) It is required only for narrators who are not upright.
D) It is recommended but not required.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, knowledge of linguistic meaning is a required condition for narrator acceptance because accurate preservation depends on understanding, and without it, suspicion remains.
